=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
The idea is clever — maybe more clever for the publisher looking for a line extension than for the players wanting an RPG card game — but no matter, as this is a quality production that I think will earn its share of fans. What Paizo has done is to repurpose their Rise of the Runelords art for a cooperative fantasy card game that uses quasi-Pathfinder mechanics to quasi-reproduce the events and adventures of their Runelords Adventure Path.

The result is a polished-if-mechanical exercise in flipping cards and rolling dice, with a little bit of RPG flavor filtering in via table talk and rationalizations about what is happening (“That dog pissed on my leg at the waterfront!”). S’okay. The clever bit is that this is a sort of slow-motion deck-building game, with character decks and abilities evolving over a continuing campaign … but after playing it twice last night, I’m not sure I see myself playing this game often enough to explore its secrets, so I haven't rated it here (my gut is that it rates a "6," but more play might bump that number).

RATING: 8
Yera

Es un juego de cartas, deckbuilder, con mucho material para expandir (pero no es un LCG), que plantea un argumento de aventuras "espada y brujeria". 

La caracteristica diferenciadora de Pathfinder es que originalmente era un juego de rol, y esta es su conversion a juego de cartas, pero manteniendo simultaneamente la mayor parte de la jugabilidad y desarrollo del personaje del juego de rol. Por ello en el juego se utilizan dados multifaceta (de 8, 10, 12, 20 caras) proponiendo un punto de encuentro entre juego de rol y deckbuilder. 

Todo ello lo consigue. el juego es rico, variado y desafiante. 

Donde en mi opinion el juego falla, es en el argumento. El juego es un seguidor de las tematicas clasicas (D&D, ESDLA, warhammer, etc) pero no es ninguna de esas "grandes licencias"... y el juego lo que hace, es clonar todo lo clonable de aquellas, y no ofrecer practicamente nuevo a todo ello. Es "espada y brujeria" super generico y no ofrece (o no he visto que ofrezca) nada novedoso o diferente en ello. 

En consecuencia, yo la sensacion que tuve jugando, fue estar jugando a un clon de D&D/ESDLA "sin chicha". no encontré la propuesta del juego inmersiva, y por ello pasó sin pena ni gloria durante la tarde y en mi pensamiento. 

Probablemente merezca una segunda partida por mi parte. 

marcado por yera para venta en 05/2017 (el juego + 6 expansiones)

RATING: 7
This is a really interesting take on the cooperative D&D genre of board games.  Although it does not stray far from its roots mechanically, it does strip out many of the mechanics and thematic elements which make a lot of these games pretty tedious.  For example, movement is completely abstracted, and combat does not have any tactical component.  A pretty robust party building and character/equipment building game is still there using the typical fantasy tropes.  You want to assemble a party with a rogue to deal with traps, a fighter to kill monsters, and then you need to work together to delve through these different dungeons and find the villain.  

I was very much surprised and had a much better time than I expected playing this game.  We played the suggested basic scenario with the suggested basic decks and still had a good time.  I suspect with expansions improving the card mix and adding more interesting adventures, plus the possibility of campaign style play, this could grow to an 8 rating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pathfinder: Rise of the Runelords finally delivers on a deckbuilding game with a campaign system. Ever since Dominion, this kind of thing has seemed like a natural fit. But games like Descent 2nd Edition and Mage Knight really just added a minor deckbuilding element to an adventure game instead of making it the focus. While Pathfinder’s the quick playing time and prosaic scenarios don’t allow for strong thematic integration, the mechanics themselves provide real drama as players work together, take risks, and (occasionally) suffer crushing defeat. 

 This is a game that you have to commit to. If you aren’t willing to buy all the expansions, you really aren’t getting the whole game. And that’s a big financial outlay. Yet for the amount of content, it is pretty reasonable. Every scenario has a unique villain and many of the henchman are only used in a single scenario as well. New cards are added and removed all the time so your encounters have great variety. And seeing a coveted item appear creates brutal choices about whether to put resources into getting it (you may never see it again) or to focus on winning the scenario at hand. Those kinds of metagame-centered choices are the heart of this game, and are really only possible when each scenario is considered in the context of the overall adventure path. 

As cooperative games go, it is not challenging enough (losses are sporadic and death is even rarer). As deckbuilders go, there is none of the engine-building or combo potential that often exists (players have little control over what specific cards they acquire). As pseudo-RPGs go, there isn’t enough thematic integration (players can find an aquatic monster in a farmhouse or defeat a person with a lock pick). And yet in spite of all of that, this game hits a sweet spot for me of accessibility combined with the fun of progressing a character and adventuring as a team.  It’s as addicting as it is delightful.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I bought PACG to play solitaire, and unfortunately, it fell flat for me compared to other solo games I like. The decisions the game presents you with are mostly either obvious or not very important. It's more a game where you observe what happens, than one where you actively affect the outcomes. 

It's a shame, because there are lots of things I still like about the game:

* Your character can evolve between games and gain new powers, or update his/her attributes. This creates a good feeling of being on an actual long term adventure.

* Cards for your deck are acquired through playing as opposed to simply selecting them from a common pool like you do in LotR LCG, for example. This, like the previous point, really contributes to the game feeling like an adventure, and it also makes each card you acquire more salient in your mind. 

* The different barriers (obstacles to overcome that are not monsters) usually have different ways to be handled depending on whether your character excels in magic, acrobatics, endurance, etc.

* The different ways you can use cards -- burying under you character, discarding, recharging by putting at the bottom of your deck, banishing into the box, etc. -- seem to offer lots of possibilities to make the game more decision heavy and is a great mechanic.

* Another nice mechanic is your deck serving as your hit points. It's an interesting balancing act and I really like it.

* The stylized artwork is quite fresh unlike that of most fantasy games that take themselves too seriously.

* The adventures and adventure paths that give you a feel of continuity and being part of a story.

Even with all these great things, there is just not enough game here for me. My major gripes with PACG:

* The flip-and-roll mechanics quickly grew stale for me. This is something that could've perhaps been remedied by varied and complex card effects, but the card effects usually have to do with adding more dice to your roll, which makes them feel uninteresting and repetitive. Even the different ways in which you can use cards that I mentioned in my list of likes, differ primarily in how much you add to your roll.

* The way the scenarios are constructed -- out of all cards with variation only in the locations, villain and henchmen -- makes the scenarios feel samey. Contrast this with some of the quests in LotR LCG where there are different stages in which the objectives and rules change, and which can feel very different.

I really wanted to like PACG, but ultimately decided it doesn't work for me and traded it away. It is a good attempt at making a thematic, RPG-like, adventure card game, but it falls short in the gameplay department.

RATING: 6
Well got a play in with 5 players, first off, NOT my idea playing with 5, to me this is a 2 to 4 player game with 3 probably best. 5 works well enough mechanically (though why did the designer choose to NOT scale the timer for any number other than 4, ball drop!?) but it becomes too long and though you CAN interact when not your turn, it still feels like downtime to me.

I guess some flavour text, while being the weakest form of theme integration, would of actually been very suitable and helpful here considering this is supposed to be a card game version of a RPG. (not an RPG by any stretch)

Basically all my thoughts below did not change especially in my 3rd edit, due to watching the videos it played exactly as I expected, so still torn, also. Owning Warhammer Quest I am not sure I need a game like this, as playing this just makes me want to play the better version with WHQ, however, they are probably different enough. I think the thing that bothers me the most out of the 3 big negatives below is the repetition, much like Castle Ravenloft the simplicity while being a "Pro" in one sense, is also its biggest weakness. I would just like to see something mechanically other than flip a card, and do a skill check, maybe expansions could spice it up somewhat, or fan-made houserules if not, but I think flipping a card and rolling every turn is going to get old REALLY fast for me.   

===================

Damn, I got all excited until I seen DB co-op game, which also sounds a little like co-op Thunderstone. I do wonder if there are enough differences, and if the D&D/RPG elements and theme could still suck me in. The mini-charts on the cards ruin the art and look of the card somewhat, maybe its still the best way though

Edit - Watched the first game play video, its mostly like  Gloom of Kilforth for a comparison, with just a touch of Thunderstone and LOTR-LCG. Though it plays very differently, it fits in the same slot as Ravenloft/Drizzt series. Probably not RPG-ish enough for me, too much "go here, see what is there, and then roll a die and see what happens", rinse, repeat until game ends (that is all there is actually, but at the end you make a couple choices on what cards to keep in your deck for next game aka "leveling" if you win). Decisions look too straight-forward, and it seems no bigger story developing so likely going to pass. 

The best part of the game does look to be the aspect similar to Mage Knight, where leveling means you improve your deck, MK is much more of a game than this one from what I see though. 

Edit 2 - There could be more story here developing than was shown in the first video, at least when you consider playing a campaign. LOTR-TCG's theme really gets overshadowed by the mechanics for me (only played twice however), so I will hope theme comes through more here. The main draw for me here is the campaign aspect, and leveling characters, and as an old-time D&D/RPG player it might just suck me in yet. I still think WHQ (with houserules) is leagues above this one, but this looks pretty easy to set up and play in comparison. Should watch a more in-depth video or better yet, play it

Edit 3 - Watched a couple more vids. Looks very well done overall, fairly thematic and D&D-like (for a card game) and potentially addictive kind of fun as you want to level/improve your character (something not enough other games have done). Negatives are the big dose of luck, highly repetitive/lack of variation, and decisions are pretty minimal (Last two bother me the most) 

Very torn, those are some big negatives, yet there is a 'draw' there for me with my tastes/background for sure. Likely a 6 for me if there was no campaign system, and maybe a 7 with it, borderline for a purchase for me.

RATING: 5
(Comment made when only the base set, 1st adventure, and character add-on are available. 5 plays.)

This is a decently fun, light, adventure game. Not as brain taxing or rewarding of cleverness as LotR LCG, but fun in a beer-and-pretzels way. One aspect that is really fun are the persistent character decks across subsequent games. The RPG aspect of "here's a pile of loot, who wants what?" has been captured very nicely here.

So far the adventures are very straight forward. Chase a villain around and trap him. More variation is supposedly coming, but we don't know the details of that yet.

There is a high luck factor here. If the villains and his henchmen float to the top of their respective decks, you'll have a much easier time than if they all hide at the bottom. There are a lot of dice rolls, and many instances where you're pulling a random monster from among all possible monsters.

The main decisions to make revolve around the blessing and ally cards. These usually give you two choices for their use: help a character defeat an enemy or help the party get to the villain faster (and you are on a turn clock). The game distills some key RPG concepts with a very simple combat system, and grafts that onto a press-your-luck card game.

Go into it looking for a fun monster smash, and you'll probably find it. Especially if you're a sucker for adventure games like I am. Go into it looking for the complexity and rewarding gameplay of Magic Realm, LotR,  or even Mage Knight and you'll probably be disappointed.

Initial Rating: 7.0 (October 2013)

I like card games, lately I have delved into, Mage Wars, Android: Netrunner, Call of Cthulhu LCG and Star Wars LCG, so it is no wonder that I like this game. I thought I wasn’t going to like it because it is a CoOp game, but I did. Perhaps, I need to review my opinion of LOTR LCG, another CoOp game I had no intention of playing before :D (and they are so close in the BGG´s ratings right now).

On the plus side, the fantasy setting is great, I love the way the scenarios are stacked on, they really told a story. You can customize your deck, The game is easily expandable (as shown by the huge box). The rules are easy to grasp and the gameplay is fast paced. 

On the bad side, the art could have been a lot better, it is adequate but subpar IMO. The setup can be a little annoying for some people (not me) and by far, my main complaint, it is way too easy with 2 players. I think with 4 players the game can be harder BUT I don’t really want to play it with 4 players (due to the downtime), perhaps with 3. I hope there is an official way to play with a harder difficulty setting. I have not read the rules yet, but I will to look for it. I’m not sure about the replayablity factor here, and that’s the main reason I have not bought the game. I would not want to play the same scenario over and over.

Bottom line, a good game that I want to play soon. I will probably buy it, but I’m not sure about the replay factor. I didn’t knew about Pathfinder before this game. As the game is kind of light, I’m not sure it will stand the test of time for me, but right now I’m loving it. 

Current Rating: 7.0

RATING: 6

Not the best game in the world but definitely has a place in my collection.

RATING: 6
2014-11-15 - I was surprised by how much I enjoyed this. Yes, on the surface, it seems light on decisions, but there's a bit more to it when you get into it. The tensions arises from the fact that your deck is your health, and if you ever run out of cards, you're dead. So, do you discard that card to use its ability or buff your power? Or do you hang on to it and risk discarding even more when you take damage? It's a good system. I'd play again.

2015-11-10 - I'm dropping my rating from a 7 to a 6, not because there's anything wrong with the game, just because my interest in playing it has waned a bit.

RATING: 7
If you're willing to tolerate occasional thematic oddities (a Hill Giant on a boat or a Collapsed Ceiling in the woods) and situational quirks in the mechanics (contradictory or impossible card interactions), this is a very enjoyable game. It tells a story and provides an adventure feel as well as cards alone can. If you want to scratch the RPG itch without appointing a dungeon master, preparing an adventure beforehand, or setting up a more complex dungeon crawl game, this is a solid way to go. The story gets progressively darker, which diminished the enjoyment a bit for me; and it runs perhaps a bit too long. Don't get me wrong--it's a fun game, but by the end of the final adventure, I was happy to be done.

RATING: 4
I love Pathfinder, but I'm surprised at how well rated this game is.  After about 5 games, I was ready to put this one away for good.

Essentially you turn over a card. it gives you a number to beat using a specific (or multiple) stat(s), and you roll a die against that number to see if you can beat it.  The only "strategy" that comes into play is whether another character blesses the roll or not.  Seriously, that's pretty much it.   

Keep the TV on during this game, as a lot of players will be sitting around waiting. . .

RATING: 7
You have a hand of cards made up of various weapons, armor, and spells, blessings, items, and allies. You go through stacks of cards to fight and add new cards to your hand. You are in different locations, but can help each other by playing certain cards or using special abilities. This seemed odd since you are not in the same place. Also you need to randomly select cards to your hand limit. This means you have good stuff with you, but it is random what you can use. Then there is the randomness of the dice rolling, but by working together we seemed to be able to roll more dice than needed. Yet after all of those weeknesses there was some fun in beating monsters or villains and in collecting new cards. It was the progrssion and teamwork which made this fun. I just think dungeon crawling games still have so much more potential.

RATING: 4
A dull game with a large set up time and theme problems. You're more or less totally unable to influence another party member's encounter without special abilities which feels so completely wrong in practice. The number of times our barbarian was standing next to our bard watching him get squashed by a zombie...

Worst of all, massive quality control issues both known and purposely left unaddressed by Paizo. Chinese printers produced colour variations in some of the sheets such that the backs of the cards were noticeably different making it hard not to know what you're about to draw. If you got one of the faulty sets then Paizo would replace them. However they decided to move to US printers part way through the adventure path which were totally different colours to the Chinese ones. If you had a Chinese set then Paizo will not replace these with new US prints, you will just be expected to deal with it. In other words they traded a situation where you would sometimes have a problem and Paizo would sort it out, for a situation where you were guaranteed to have a problem that Paizo would do nothing about. Customer services will direct you to the Paizo forums where their dedicated fans will fervently defend the company and advise you to buy new cards or sleeves. Spend more money on Paizo after this? How about I never buy anything from Paizo ever again instead?

RATING: 8
I've played this quite a bit now (66 plays), I haven't played the last pack of adventures yet. It's hard to argue with the play count, clearly something has kept us coming back to play this game. Even for the combined price 66 plays is great bang for the buck. However, the game does leave quite a lot to be desired. The basic mechanic is kind of boring. To win you must make sure not to spend cards on much else other than exploring and defeating Henchmen and Villains. Spread out to cover the areas, optimize people's placement based on which locations they can close, rinse and repeat ad nauseam. When you do fight someone who matters you have so many dice that you completely obliterate them. There are not many interesting decisions, and there's no tension.

So... 66 plays!! Why? Well, I think there is one aspect of the game which really shines and that's the RPG-esque character progression. It's just so satisfying to level up and get better loot. It's unfortunate that the game you play between these level ups is a bit dull and underdeveloped. It has been fun to see how the different characters play and experiment with different builds (to the extent that it's possible). 

Certainly not a stellar game, and hardly deserving of the 8 I rate it, but I just can't argue with the amount we've played the game. Definitely the worst game I've ever rated an 8 if that makes any sense.

------------------------------------------------------

So far this game has been very addictive and a lot of fun. It isn't a Euro-game by a long shot but it is a wonderful combination of RPG and card game. The most engaging feature is the long-term character progression including the slow procedure of deck building. 

It is a unique game system which I think will appeal to a lot of people. If you don't mind dice too much and like the theme of the game you should be inclined to enjoy this. My group has a strong preference for low-luck euro games but we all have some RPG background. We played this 5 times in a row consuming 9 hours and our entire weekly game day. For us this is incredibly unusual. We're going to arrange a separate day to play this for the foreseeable future.

RATING: 8

RATING: 7
Boardgames can't get much closer to an RPG than PACG. Pass skill checks, use weapons, items and allies to improve your chances, improve your equipment and explore your environment. The rulebook isn't the best leading to repeated rules queries in the first plays. Once past the initial rules hurdles, a pleasant flow should be achievable.

PACG works well as an experience game. Exploring is fun, managing your hand isn't too challenging but nice. The fights are not too complicated. Theme-wise, it succeeds.

Mechanically, the game is average. The variance is limited with the same goal for each scenario to nail down a changing villain by locking all escape routes before defeating him in battle. The difficulty doesn't scale to well across the player count. Surviving the campaign solo doesn't seem possible. Finding the villain can be a chore with two players as he has lots of opportunities to escape. We'll see how far we get with two players. Four players purportedly is too easy. The difficulty of the fights is odd. Defeating the villain and the henchmen can be a piece of cake, defeating certain monsters can be well-nigh impossible if the heroes of the group lack the right skills.

A potentially good game that has to prove if the provided experience compensates the many weaknesses.

RATING: 7.5
An RPG-style game comprising entirely of cards. The gameplay is a bit too simple. Every turn, flip a card and roll a check. Use cards to modify the roll and thats it. On the other hand, the brilliance of it is that characters can be easily stored from game to game.

I really feel something could be improved. A few symbols for recurring text (e.g. weapons reveal or adventure difficulty). Not really sure how I feel about this game yet.

RATING: 7.5
I grabbed this during my endless search for the one true dungeon crawler.  There's no map or minis, but one can get a dungeon crawler vibe in a theater of the mind aspect.  While it didn't QUITE scratch the dungeon crawler itch it at least put a little ointment on it.  If you just go through the mechanics of the game, it will not feel thematic or at all like a dungeon crawl.  In order to feel the theme and get a story going, when playing with my wife we had a rule that for each card revealed we had to tell a story about what was unfolding.  It made for a lot of fun and it became somewhat of a story telling game prompted by the cards and dice.  It does eventually get a bit repetitive though as the basic scenario mechanic is essentially corner and defeat the villain 90% of the time.  Still, it made for a fun time.

RATING: 8.9
Very clever abstraction of an RPG into a card game. Maybe a little too abstracted though. Can start to feel repetitive depending on the character you're playing. 

Playing every scenario once through the entire campaign takes 39 plays. Playtime for each is about 45-90 minutes so multiple scenarios can be played in a night. This will happen more times than not and you will want to keep going, play "just one more". This can lead into the wee hours of the night, but it's enjoyable and something that not many other games can achieve.

As for replayability, I'm not sure there is much. Taken as a whole, I've played it through once; it just took 41 sessions (we lost twice). But now that I've gotten to the end of the campaign and beaten the big baddy, I don't really have a desire to go through it again any time soon.
Be all that as it may, I really can't think of any other game that I've gotten over 40 hours of play out of. Well, there was that one time we played Talisman.

RATING: 8
The iOS app is superior in every way to the tabletop game if you're playing this solo/2p. The tabletop game is a chore of setup/teardown time and calculating lots of modifiers. Also, Paizo printed a different backing on the adventure decks for RotR, which is really poor QC, as you can always tell which are the expansion cards. 

I love the app and the game design for solo and 2p pass-n-play.

RATING: 3
Not my kind of game. Maybe it's yours, I dunno. Our scenario, replete with discoveries that we played things incorrectly (common with cooperative games since everyone has to play against the game), ended up being a 3 and a half hour ordeal. Since each person only had 6 turns - the scenario was 30 turns and we played with 5 people - this means that one would have to wait about a half-hour before their turn was next. While I believe I played with some people that really slowed things down I also think that this is the kind of game that will see a strong positive skew towards gameplay, as people go through their hands and see what sort of support is available for others. 

I've played plenty of dungeon-crawlers that attempt to emulate role-playing games: the worst IMO are those, like Descent First Edition and this game, that fetishize the dice-rolling and combat poorly: in Descent one played a dungeon-crawler by avoiding combat as much as possible and equipping fast players with double shields. In this game characters will split up from each other and hide from combat as much as they are able - since killing most monsters again yields nothing.

I don't see what most people see in this. If the goal is to play a dungeon-crawl that doesn't need to be set up as one, play Tales of the Arabian Knights. If one wants some dice-rolling combat, just play 4th edition D&D: it's probably quicker.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The concept is great. But after 6 scenarios played, the game falls flat for me. 

1. Every session of play is kind of the same.

2. The luck of the dice vs. what card comes up seems to primarily determine whether your party wins a scenario or not. There is some player skill involved, but, well, not that much. (Really just getting to know the cards in your deck and cycling through your deck at the right speed.)

3. So many items, allies, armor, spells, weapons - and so little of value that your character ends up with. 

4. Finding the Villain, defeating him and then having him escape...catching him again and him escaping again. Just how fun is that?

5. Some characters are much better at repeatedly going through a location than others. (So some players play significantly longer.)

6. Some cards require lots of luck for them to be of any use. Example: a card that gives you a bonus die for a Fortitude check - you might have 2 of these in a scenario and then not encounter a Fortitude check for 4 or more scenarios...you have that useless card in your deck. 

7. After playing through three scenarios the game becomes routine, with the excitement level petering out to nothing.

It's a shame as this game has a lot of cool things about it.

RATING: 5
This got a fair bit of solo & group play but it's replay-ability is low, driving one to buy additional content.

The desire for self-preservation for each player does a good job at ensuring you don't have one bossy player telling everybody what to do which tends to happen in most co-op games.

As players build their deck from scenario to scenario the "good" items end up in the players' decks and you're driven to buy more content to continue building up items.

The game can be a definite money sink but it's fast to teach and good to pull out when you have a group of friends that have never played it.

Update:  I now own the Lord of the Rings living card game starter set and I'd recommend that one over this for more replayability and cheaper price.

========================================================
FIRST IMPRESSION:

I think I want to like this more than I actually do right now.  There is definitely some cleverness within the game as you seek to level up your character(s), the life mechanism with your deck, and the use of all the different dice certainly brings in the swingyness and chaos of dungeon delving.  Right now, we're probably thinking too much and not enjoying the experience, but I do like the concept of player leveling and each one brings in a different play experience.  

The setup is really a pain as it takes a bit of time setting up each scenario, but I suppose this could be much worse.  Card stock leaves a bit to be desired.  Box is huge.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It is an interesting game, but falls short because of several overall "inconsistencies" (at least for my personal taste):
1) It fails in achieving what it claims to achieve - bringing RPG onto the "board" (=as a card game). It is more of a math-optimization procedure than an adventure/role-playing game. The scenarios are somewhat generic, always finding the villain and defeating him, with varying "locations" (= some special rule and a different composition of generic weapon, item, monster, ally etc cards).
2) For the complexity of interactions between different cards and character skills it still always boils down to a roll of dice, sometimes with really strong impact. Although one could always argue that the amount of dice rolling should somewhat even-out the luck, this is in every other occasion not the case. For my taste, this is too thinky a game to be that much dependent on the luck.
3) It just feels dry - no story-driving elements, always fighting, lose-or-win, etc.

So, I am disappointed by the game with regard to what it supposes to achieve, although the game itself is ok.

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
On one hand I really like the progression in this game as you build your deck and gain abilities from adventure to adventure. It definitely gives an RPG feel, especially with each scenario having decks specific to it (there isn't much of a narrative, but e.g. the academy location is full of spell cards and if you ever explore a non-spell, you get to explore again). On the other hand, this game is just skill check after skill check. Sure, there is hand management of should you discard this card or not for this check or save it for later, but it feels really repetitive at times. Also, taking damage that wipes out your whole hand can be devastating, as the cards you draw afterwards may very well not be what you need to face future skill checks, making a comeback difficult (besides wiping out over a third of your health), and I hate that this game has fallaway loser/runaway leader (when encountering a bane/acquiring a necessary boon, wasting time on failing to defeat it would be bad enough, but not only is there damage, but a lot of scenarios punish you even more e.g. lose 3 turns on the game's timer. For runaway leader there is a lot of barriers let you explore again if you pass, and a lot of locations give you items if you close them, besides the saving time by progressing and not taking damage).

RATING: 3
Encounters are random and cooperation minimal. I really wanted to like it, but it almost feels like a solitaire game flipping cards face up on a deck.

The main problem is the almost complete lack of meaningful decisions: move my character to a location (which I know the type of contents at that location, but the differences are pretty minor, like 2 weapons instead of 1). I flip a card face up and then I need to deal with it.

If it's not a monster, chances are it's an item that particular character has no interest in (like your fighter getting a spell, or your ranger getting a big axe). Even if another character would love to get that item, it's too bad because the character that needs to do the check isn't proficient in that (I understand every character can discard a blessing to add a die, but you can't do that for all the good items, especially if you need to save them for the really important stuff).

Or maybe it's an ally which gives you bonuses for some skill that you have no idea if you'll need to use or not in the future. But you're lucky and you get the ally.

At that point you're over your hand limit, so you might as well discard the ally to explore again and repeat the cycle. Eventually you'll get to the henchmen, or the villain and you'll corner him and win. Or not.

RATING: 9.4
I watched this game closely for almost a year before I finally bought.  I read so many over-the-top, positive reviews for it that I became skeptical that it was being grossly over-hyped.  Now that I've got several plays under my belt I can honestly say that the reviews were spot on.

This is an awesome game.  The box is loaded with content, putting other card games to shame.  It comes with 3 sort of prequel scenarios (which can be played multiple times) and loads of cards for everything else you need to create and start questing with your hero.  That's before you even open the 6 additional scenarios in the first adventure pack that comes with the game.

A lot of people have compared this game to an MMORPG or Diablo.  Yes, it does have that addictive, "raid dungeon" mentality that will pull you along, tempting you to enter the dungeon over and over again for a piece of loot you want.  But even with the game's addictive nature, the game-play is really good.  I've never had so much fun in a co-op board game working together with the other players.  That you can carry your character throughout the journey just adds to the fun.

I could go on and on, but I can't recommend this enough for anyone who likes card, board or role-playing games.  The whole story is covered in all 6 scenarios and I recommend getting them all including the character add-on deck for the complete experience.

Variants I recommend are the Linear Movement system and the Adventure Guide PDF.  It adds to the story-telling aspect and takes nothing away from the game-play.  Also check out my random adventure generator variant.  This makes the game endlessly re-playable after you finish the storyline.

RATING: 7
Though it is nowhere near to provide the stuff I like in Roleplaying games, it feeds the same strange hunger that computer games like Diablo fulfill/exploit: go against random colorful foes and collect random shiny stuff. There is just enough occasion for nifty combos that it does not fill totally mindless, although I gotta admit that I expect the difficulty to ramp up a bit after the introductory trio of scenarios. In spite of going with a somewhat lop-sided quartet (no arcane spell-user, no ranged weapon guy), we never came close to losing a team member, let alone a scenario.

We’ll see, but the fact that I’m ready to explore this further already is very high praise for a cooperative game. Playing with closed hands already keeps it from simply being a puzzle, but the sheer chaos inherent in the game design might help even more to ban the alpha player syndrome … if there’s obviously not a lot to plan for, you might as well go with the guts of the active player.

RATING: 8
a little bland and boring at first, but once you either play solo with multiple characters or with other players it really starts to shine and perform as advertised. It really is like a RPG experience with less overhead.

Artwork is mostly well done, mechanics are interesting and show plenty to build on, choices can be difficult, and the persistent character setup grows on you and makes each quest more interesting. If you might lose, you try to stay alive and just get out of a quest with some loot.

The deck building per character is actually nice though sometimes it feels limiting...but as your character grows, so too does the deck, and therefore your configurations can change.

Just a lot to love about this game and it plays GREAT solo. (recommend playing 3 characters solo)

Keep it coming!

RATING: 7.5
Wow!  What a solid game.  It really comes across as a lite D&D (or Pathfinder) style adventure without a DM.  The entire scenario is put together through a rather unique system put in place to build decks for towns, caves, and all other manner of adventurous places.  Then each character journeys through these places trying to uncover the mysteries and ultimately battling the main bad guy to win the day.

My gaming group doesn't do much D&D because it can be a strain on not only our time but on the DM as well who has to do a lot of front loading.  This game has the look and feel of an rpg without all that "mess".  I'll absolutely play this one again!

Flip a card, add a bunch of dice, roll much higher than needed, flip the next card.  I rarely felt that an encounter was a challenge and I became bored with nearly every exploration/turn feeling just like the last.  Very few Adventure Packs truly feel different from the next, with the typical exception of one Scenario per Adventure Pack that is unique in some way.

For me, this would've been better with a much shorter Adventure Path, mixing all the unique Scenario mechanics into a shorter play experience that would allow for more constant variety.  I also feel that such a format would give the game more re-playability (since I don't see myself ever going through again with a different character for only about 10% of the game to actually feel different).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I played this the first time because, although I didn't think it would be my cup of tea, I was curious about it. I like the concept. I played it the second two times because my friends were into it, and it was a fun way to hang out and get in some gaming with them. It's not my jam.

Mechanically, it's fine. There's a solid engine under the hood. But it's dull as ditchwater. Every adventure, at least in the arc we played, feels basically the same. My deck and character improve slightly each time, but there's a lot less deckbuilding than I was hoping for.

I wanted to feel immersed in the experience, and I never did. I wanted to face tough choices, but only got easy ones -- and non-choices. The D&D Adventure Game series already does everything I wanted this game to do.

I like how you can upgrade your characters after each adventure. That gives great immersion by watching your character develop as your adventure progresses. It really makes me feel like i'm playing an RPG, without the intimidation of having to create a character myself. I kinda wish there was a miniature or something else to look at though. But this is amazing considering its just a card game. And i thought the character health being portrayed as the player deck size was a really well done mechanic.

RATING: 5
Good: 
The game uses artwork from the Pathfinder universe, which I am a fan of. 

Bad
There isn't enough of a benefit to really be co-operative on another player's turns. If you help allies, you discard cards that you will need for your turn. This assures that you won't be able to as much on your turn. 

On your turn there aren't many real options, you just continue to search the location you are in until you find a challenge and then use the cards in your hand to overcome it. 

Card quality isn't as good as I would like. One character card was briefly placed on a wet spot and ended up warped. 

Overall, for me, this doesn't feel like an adventure, it feels like crawling through a location decks and hoping you don't find the main boss until you are ready for them.

RATING: 3
 Setup time is ok. Only concern is that the theme slowly disappears because decisions are primarily mathy rather than thematic in nature. In that way it reminds me of the LOTR LCG because after a few turns theme disappears and its just a math puzzle. The main thing that moves this design further is the added motivation of collecting loot (expanding your deck), this encourages players to think in character more and to be driven by their characters long term selfish needs and wants.

Update: I've gotten very fast at setting up the game so that's no longer an issue. I also found some areas were I was playing wrong so I now find the game more balanced and challenging. With the higher challenge level it starts to feel more thematic. I will be playing my first multiplayer game tomorrow and I hope that it really brings the character moments to the forefront, solo play tends to be a mathy experience because there is no one to interact with in a meaningful way...

Update: the game didn't go over well with my gaming group and its very random when played solo. This game may be dead to me... I'll give it one my chance.

Update: stopped playing solo when I stopped caring about the random story. I'll try it again with gaming group... If they aren't blown away by it I'm going to cancel my subscription.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]100,000 Feet.[/b]

First attempt at rating, after playing through the 3 scenario mini-adventure path with Valeros. 

7.5 (This is subject to change.)

Well it's a lot of fun in the short term. Convincing enough that I have bought the character expansion and full scenario set (Really I think these should come with the base set or at least be packaged into two parts rather than 6). 

I'm not totally convinced that I won't wake up one day and find that the game has turned to dust in my hands. Definitely a love it or hate it, group dependent game. All in, right now: [b]7.5[/b]

Spent an enjoyable afternoon making may way through the mini-adventure path and writing it up. Really enjoyed getting and selecting the loot. There is decision making here but I suppose it's not particularly deep and is subject random event which make it's meaningfulness limited. Still it is a light game so none of these factors are damming, I could see this being a good family game. Interpreting the cards into a story is enjoyable childish play.


[b]Very Early early thoughts (Prior to reading rules or playing the game):[/b]

The artwork and overall presentation is a heck of a lot better than I had been expecting, given the criticisms I heard. Sure the items do have a oddly clinical backdrop, not unlike the display in Skyrim. Totally serviceable and frankly it just makes the other images shine all the shinier.

Given the price I can't help but think the dice should be a little more so. (I suppose just having 'fancy dice' would be a boom for non-rpgers - meh I'll be throwing in a couple other dice sets.)

Slightly worried that the gameplay and story will be clunky and disjointed; resulting in the overall experience being lacklustre. Kinda wish there was more flavour text.

Bought an erasable ink pen specially for the set. Didn't shop around and so it ended up costing 7.5% of the base set. Sleeves should have been included, which would have resolved this and removed any delay in getting the game to the table. (Oh I see that they are in Skully Shackles = well good.)

[b]Early thoughts (Having played a dry run)[/b]

Quite a lot of text on the cards makes it a little bit tricky with soft new players unless the host is experienced. 

Odd bit of confusion with how locations and monsters powers interact.

Don't fully understand how significant banishing cards is. 

Odd bit where a 'survive' check could be used but neither PC had mention of such a variable. 

Odd bit when a character isn't proficient with weapons but starts with them... ermmm?

Feel that there should be a simple card crib sheet telling players the turn order. The games really simple but providing a visual overlay of this would be a positive step with new players.

Lem got killed half way through the game from fairly innocuous monsters. This is good but ho-hum we negated as much risk as possible and still got him killed. Hmmmmm...

We enjoyed taking turn about placing, explaining what happened in the context of game in role-play. Ah now Boris the burglar it turns out isn't a nice fellow and has decided he wishes to relieve you for your jewels and eyes (Traitor Monster). This was fun, though is group dependent.

[b]Later Early thoughts (Having played the first mini=adventure)[/b]

Yeah I've enjoyed it so far.

Feel that much of the game relies upon the enthusiasm of the audience. That's not a bad things and I suppose RPGs do in general. But, this of  course is not an RPG so it's a risky rode to take. Time will tell.

Really excited to mix in the first chapter of the scenario. Really geeky OCD fun -I Like it! 

[b]Late later early thoughts:[/b]

Well in my solo campaign I'm oddly stuck on the second mission of the official quest line. 

On paper it should be easy and is just an opportunity to get loot, in practice I've failed the mission 4 times in a row. Down to widely difficult cards draws or bizarrely low rolls - hmmmmmm. I've dropped my rating to a 7.

RATING: 8

UPDATE 20/12/16:

The app is amazing, but I did enjoy the feel of shuffling the cards and marking up my card (sleeves) as I levelled up. Lots of downtime in multiplayer and just didn't work with our group, so this makes a great solo game, but once I'd finished the RotR campaign I didn't feel the need to go back to it again. I also didn't like how the encounters and boons sort of arbitrarily wandered off if you didn't defeat them, and that you could sometimes draw "miss a turn" hands that you couldn't do anything with. That said it's an excellent innovation for introducing campaign rules into games, which is what everyone loves these days of course, and the sheer amount of content that Paizo has thrown into this series is quite staggering.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Edit: Game almost ruined for me because of poor quality control on Paizo's part.  This caused certain adventure decks to have different coloration on the card backs and to be slightly different sizes.  For a game that depends on card anonymity and lots of shuffling, this is a HUGE problem.  I refuse to spend money of sleeves for what is clearly a problem on Paizo's part. 

Main Comment:
My main comparison to this game is Runebound.  To my mind, Runebound is still the better solo adventure board game.  The maps. The sense of a journey that only just happens to be part of a larger conflict.  Runebound has very simple mechanics and a loose enough imposed story that players can, if they are so inclined, create their own story from the bits and pieces that they accumulate / encounter.  The game's "looseness" and map encourage this. 

However, what Pathfinder Adventure Card game does ABSOLUTELY better than any other game is evoke the emotions of and sensibilities of a VIDEO RPG. Lots of small, bite-sized "levels." Transitions from scenario to scenario.  Lots of "checks" for characters to engage in.  Mechanics for characters to play off of one another.  Relatively quick set up, so that a loss/death can be quickly restarted at the last "save point."  And a clean, clean attention to bookkeeping.  Honestly, I think this is BETTER than most DnD-type sessions I played in.

Also, as far as character development in terms of a "skill tree" or "buffs" the Pathfinder Adventure game is VERY impressive.  A character's deck changes with the game, and decisions get made as to how to specialize characters in terms of their skill set.  I wouldn't say that any of this necessarily lends itself to NARRATIVE, but it IS very compulsive.  It's also very satisfying to have buffed up character in terms of complimentary  abilities, so that they work well together as a team.  That is where the game really shines, imo.

That said, having played through the entire adventure path, there is an advantage to playing "smart" as opposed to "narratively."  I played with Harsk and Valeros, and I had established a running gag in which Valeros needed to prove how smart he was.  So he took on a headband that gave him extra intelligence, an ally that gave him INT/WIS, a robe that boosted his intelligence...  Similarly, Harsk was constantly missing out on allies due to his horrible charisma.  There was a gag where he took on a potion, and used it to gain an elven beauty side-kick.  Otherwise, though, it was all crows and monkeys for him.  These "narrative" decisions mostly ended up being relatively inconsequential as far as game play went in the first 4 adventure decks.  After that, though, these decisions actively hurt the characters.  Characters will perform better if you look through and adventure deck and get a sense of what sorts of checks are going to be needed, and then play / acquire things accordingly.  I lost one scenario simply because both of my characters were in a location that got hit by an avalanche.  Bad rolls by Valeros despite burning all our our blessings did us in.  Thematic?  Perhaps.  But a little frustrating, and taken care of once I stopped "building" my characters narratively.

I have to say that I found the rulebook to be surprisingly bad.  Really bad because the terminology is overly precious in certain cases and then general in others.  The rules could have been efficiently presented in half the space, and been much more useful.  DOWNLOAD ONE OF THE RULES SUMMARIES FROM BGG!!  Even so, there were many instances in which I needed to head to the BGG discussion boards to get clarification on rules.  I didn't really know that you could "retreat" until I was playing the last adventure deck.  I was under the impression that a maximum of TWO explores was allowed in a turn until the last adventure deck.  The notion of a turn was confusing for a while given certain cards...was it every character getting a go (no) or a single character's go (yes).  I didn't realize that a "trait" was different than a card level, and so started to remove all "basic" cards from the game, at one point, as opposed to cards with the basic *trait*.  I was never sure *when* certain cards could be played, and made a best guess, learning to basically trust EXACTLY what was written on a card.  If it did not specify a play condition, then assume it can be played whenever (as long as a card of that type had not already been played for a particular check).  Etc.  Etc.

On the other hand, by the time I got to the end of the game, it was pretty fantastic just how many cards did NOT have issues.  Further, the card distributions, on the whole, are pretty amazing.  Yes, there are a few cases in which a scenario is radically easier or harder based on the number of characters.  However, for 90% of the scenarios the method of titrating locations to number of characters worked very nicely.  Also for the most part, even though you randomly draw to create location events, most of the cards somehow fit the scenario.  Or at least could be imagined to fit the scenario.  I can't imagine the playtesting time that went into that.  Also, once the relatively simply rules are grasped (no thanks to the rulebook), you don't really need the rulebook.  The cards tell you, pretty much, exactly what to do.

In sum, VERY interesting and amazingly clever game.  Occupied me off-and-on for 4 months.  And that's saying something.  It really does capture the essence of a DnD-style RPG.  I don't think those sorts of RPGs do much, really, to tell stories.  It's all about going with what you've got to maximize a check.  That's the essence of DnD.  This gives you that minus the interminable stupid table comments that suck up so much time.  Compulsive in the way that video games can be...but more compulsion than evocation in my opinion.

Final edit: first rated this game game a 7.  After going through the entire adventure path, I'm giving it a 9 but with a HUGE caveat.  Having played through it once, the game no longer has much value for me.  I'm done.  I have no desire to try the adventure path again with different characters.  As I say, the game is more compulsive than evocative for me.  And so my 9 is in recognition of the fact that the game provided me with many, many hours of play.  Roughly 2 hours per scenario.  So something like 100 hours of time spread over many months.  After this experience, though, I'm done with the game, and don't *really* feel like I experienced a narrative.  Just consumed many enjoyable "snacks."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Rated as a 2-player, single scenario experience. This one needs to be played as a campaign.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a card game which follows the model of role playing games. You have a specific character, with specific dice values for traits. There are a number of locations represented by cards. Each turn you are exploring locations for items, spells, monsters and villians. Combat with monsters is resovled with dice, using the right dice for your character, and other players can add dice to your combat at the cost of a blessing card.  When your 15 card deck runs out and you can't refill, your character dies, and you lose all gained levels and items.  To win the scenario, generally you have to corner a villian so they can't flee to locations that are still open.

It's a fun game, especially if you are into the campaign mode of keeping characters and watching them grow. It's generally not hard to defeat the scenario. I'd like play a few more times to see what the different scenarios offer. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Quite enjoyed this one as a way to have a very light RPG/dungeon-crawl experience, but I'm not sure it's something I'd play regularly enough to justify owning.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A tightly integrated system, built around a wide range of cards.  Coming from a D&D RPG background, for me this plays more naturally than many RPG-derived board games, while also being deeper and more nuanced.
The blessings deck brings a sense of urgency as well as occasional unexpected bonuses; the player deck gives access to all facets of your character (if not always in the order you'd like); the location decks bring not only a loose narrative to the encounters, but also chances to improve yourself into the future.
I very much look forward to playing this further.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A solid mix of card game and story-based RPG. Its cards guide you through hints of a story (touching on a lot of the lore from the Pathfinder comics and rulebooks), so there's more story setup than, say, Dungeoneer. Ultimately, the game is about powering-up your character and coming up with strategies to ferret out your target villain as efficiently as possible, racing against a countdown. A fun single and multi player game.

RATING: 8
A well designed game with a HUGE replay value. This is not a deck building game like Android: Netrunner is for example. As the campaign progresses you get better gear that you can use in your character deck which is relatively small in number.

You buy new card packs (Adventures) to continue adventuring.

Pros:
- Good balance and design.
- Replay value is huge. There are many characters to choose from and how to progress with them. Location decks change every time. New cards in adventure decks.
- You can play this game alone and you can start playing with a new character anytime, even if other players have played many scenarios already, you can "hop in."
- Character advancement with story arch over multiple sessions.
- Character deck is fun to build.

Cons:
- If you are a role-player the role-playing element is at it's minimum. You could add some storytelling if you wanted. This is pretty much a hack 'n' slash game.
- The setup time between scenarios is long, you need to shuffle every card and build decks... shuffling, shuffling.
- If you want to continue playing, you eventually need to keep buying adventure decks.

After a tense and focused play (I believe that Amiri died beause I wasn't very focused on the previous game -the problems of playing when you are tired-), Merisiel executed the killing blow on Nualla avenging her friend in the process.

Pros: Adictive on campaign mode. Captures the essence of an RPG and puts it on a card game. Great on solo, interesting on multi-player (you can chose wether to help or not). Highly replayable. When deployed, fits on a desk so I can play it more often than other games.

Cons: Can be repetitive, repetitive, repetitive if played a lot. The set up takes some time and a lot of shuffling. You have to look on the FAQ or the rulebook at least once a play. 

Word of advice: This is not a deckbuilding game, but you have to "build" the locations' and your character's deck in order to play. The deckbuilding it's made all on the set up phase.

Last thoughts about the game: After 35 plays and at the beginning of the last chapter I think I exhausted the game. Though I still have fun playing it it has become a bit dull because of it's repetitiveness and I find easier every play I make. Still, I enjoyed tremendously playing this game solitaire with four characters.

Really enjoying the senario decks Paizo is putting out for this game. This game has completely replaced Sentinels of the Multiverse for me in terms of co-op card games. Further adventures may be had in different, more mechanically complex and thematic LCGs releasing Fall 2016 (See Arkham Horror: The Card Game).

We pushed through and finished our Lem and Harsk campaign - it was very challenging and exciting - I am happy we played the entire adventure, but am excited to move on after the 5 years and 2 days it took to get through all the content.  There has been a lot of innovation in the space since 2013!

BOX INSERT: Paizo factory insert is surprisingly adequate if you juggle categories around as needed.  Since the game may be shelved once completed, no commercial insert will likely be required.  

Also numerous sessions via iOS application on iPad/iPhone.  Nice to not have to setup, but cardboard is still king for multiplayer for sure.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
A fun adventure game that takes the RPG experience and removes the DM to streamline the experience. Fight monsters, get loot, chuck dice yadda yadda.

At first I really enjoyed this game. I didn't know too much about the pathfinder universe but I was drawn in by the deck building character growth aspect. Each scenario feels like a chase where you hunt down the big bad boss until you corner him. Upon multiple plays some flaws of the game began to reveal themselves.

1) Majority of scenarios play out the same! there are 3 in the base box (2 seek & destroy and 1 "disable traps")

2) the locations and general themes of the scenario get recycled and feel generic! There's no narrative really being played out. There's no reason in particular to search the forest for the big baddie- you just do it because that's how the game works. none of the scenarios have set monsters to fight against except the minions the big bad has with him in the game. the monsters are random so you can find a zombie in the middle of the city streets and a goblin at the farm.

3) Unbalanced stats use. Characters in the game have stats and skills that give bonuses to dice rolls however a lot of skills in the game are just not important. You are honestly better off just making sure everyone can fight and that you have at least 1 strength person and 1 INT/CHA person on the team.

FINAL:
Having played through the entire set of Adventure Packs 1-6. I can definitely say that this game got worse as it went on. The game gets progressively harder in terms of dice rolls but the decisions don't ever really feel meaningful. the game ends up boiling down to a soulless dice chucker that feels light on story/purpose, imbalanced in character skill use, and lacking depth.

RATING: 9
Mmmmm, Bard's Tale II in card format!

This reminds me so much of that game.  Even though the mechanics are simple (play top card from turn deck, turn over a card, calculate dice needed to give you your best odds at matching or beating a number, roll 'em, discard card or shuffle it back in the stack, rinse and repeat with some nuances until the turn deck runs out or you beat the right card), it really feels like a level up dungeon crawl to me.  A fun one!

The complaints about the turn deck do not make sense to me.  I think that is because I have been mostly playing a character (Seelah) that can really use those cards (and it sure is sweet when they line up just right).

I also don't know why people are saying it is too easy.  I seem to have about a 50% success rate so far.  I have had only one game where the villain and henchmen were in the top 3 cards (4 locations and I have been seeing about 28-35 cards per game) and that makes a huge difference.  I think certain characters are more likely to be successful (hard to argue with a d10 + 3 combat check without a weapon)  I also don't help another character unless it is at the same location.

Why this is not a 10:
- I do not want to play it all the time - too many other great games out there!

RATING: 5
I'm truly conflicted about this game.
On one hand, this was the game that marked my entry on modern boardgames. As such, it will always hold some significance.
However, while the campaign is fun for the first 1-3 adventure packs, it does start to get repetitive after a while. It does not help that it seems very combat oriented, and that the structure of the game always involves finding the villain in a very similar way. Furthermore, having played this game in a group of 3, I felt like my character - Ezren - was miles above any other character in the party, who rarely got significant loot, while I would always get an upgrade after every major encounter.

Given these two flaws, I can't rate the gamr higher than 5, even tough I would love to give it a greater. It's a great game for a certain niche of people, it just won't fit every taste.

RATING: 5.5
A [poor] attempt at turning the Adventure Paths that made Paizo great into a board game, or more precisely, into a Living Card Game.

What I really like about it is the deck management system (the deck is your life, you can bury cards, sacrifice them, etc.), and the Campaign mode [character advancement after each adventure, like RPG's campaigns].

I really wanted this to be a great game, but there are many things about I don't like:
-> Dice rolls are way too important (more important than in a RPG and way more important than in a regular board game).
-> Very low replay value (how can it be at this price and with so many cards??).
-> Some characters are unplayable in solo play.

I was on the verge of buying it when I saw it at a great price right after it was published. I'm very glad I didn't do it.

I don't see this taking the place of a good RPG or board game, but still, I'm willing to give it a try.

***********************

Edit: after trying it, I was wrong about the decisions. You actually have to take meaningful ones and there are enough of them to provide the game with some quite light tactic and strategic content. Still, it's true that most decissions are evident, depending on the characteristics of your character and the group.

Still, it's replayability is very low. Only three adventures on the base game and now that I have played one, I have almost no interest to play it again. Yes, the items and some encpounters would be different, but the key items and the atrategy will be the same. The most difference will be created by playing with different characters.

On the other side, the campaign mode is not that great. You just improve a single stat in a minor way.

I had fun during the game, I increased my rating from 4 to 5.5 (I punish very heavily the absolute lack of replayability and the very few scenarios).

RATING: 8.5
This is everything we want in an RPG without actually having to play a true RPG D&D style game with paper and pencil, we absolutely love the card mechanic and deck building in this game. Me and my wife thoroughly enjoy this lite RPG. The game plays quickly and smoothly and is a great gateway in the the RPG genre. We love the fact that we can play the 2 of us in under 60 mins and not feel like we are missing out on the experience because we don't have 4-6 players. The game seems to be extremely well balanced and well filled with content. All and all we love this dungeon delving, loot grabbing, dice rolling, check making, deck building, persistent character development coop adventure game!  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Have a character with your own deck of cards. On a turn, go to a location. Flip the top card of that location. Do its skill check by rolling the die your character tells you, and add more dice or bonuses using cards from your hands. Then either use card abilities to do more stuff or end your turn. Continue having turns around the table until someone finds a baddie at a location and defeats it, then roll a skill check to close the location down, reminiscent of Arkham Horror. When you find the big boss, he'll escape to a non-closed location, so basically you want to close stuff down until he's got nowhere to go, and then if you defeat him, you win the scenario. Next scenario, get new locations (each location has different special effects), build a new deck for each location, and go again. After each scenario, you wean your deck back to its starting configuration, but if you've found some better spells say at a location, you can choose to keep these and discard your starter spells, still getting you back to your max 3 spells or whatever. Which nicely ups your character as you work your way through scenarios which no doubt will get tougher as you go. That's quite cool. But the turns aren't much more than flip a card, roll a die. There's little co-op stuff you can do other than use cards to take some damage or add a die occasionally if you're at the same location. Happy to play again if I want to not think too much and just enjoy the company, but really it's for those who like this sort of thing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Neat system. A true-coop card game with an RPG feel and light deck-building mechanic. A little intuitive. Looking forward to more. I really like the decisions that crop up. This scratches my itch to play Runebound, Mage Knight, and Decipher's Star Wars CCG. Plays quicker than you think (except for a learning game).  Much errata and revisions, but I'm still loving this. The artwork is wonderful.

This comes with dice, but the games go faster when people bring their RPG dice along.

Probably not best with 2 player, though that is how I'm playing it mostly for now, and I'm finding it super fun. We hope to redo the paths with different character combos. I can't wait to try some paths solo.

For those not familiar to RPGs, there will be a learning curve.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
"Clever deck management fantasy adventure game with a good theme, and each hero plays quite differently. The gameplay is not as dull as some claim (it's NOT 'flip a card; roll dice; repeat'), but it's not fantastic either. The charm of this game, and the only reason it shines, is if you campaign over multiple sessions, watching your character grow, and eagerly awaiting new stuff to encounter. I went in with fairly low expectations, but it has become a favorite. My wife and I finished a campaign, and have started going through a second time. Unfortunately it doesn't really work solo with one character. After over 100 plays, and wanting more, this has become the second game I have ever rated a 10. "

What I like:
- Different characters play very differently
- Clever mechanism of cards-in-deck = hit points
- Can play quickly (learning 2p game took under an hour; second scenario took 2 hours)
- The idea of leveling up over multiple sessions
- Avoids the co-op cliche of "each turn, flip a card to advance evil"
- I'm a sucker for fantasy adventure games

What I don't like:
- Remembering the special rules for each location AND the scenario can be tricky
- There are a lot of FAQ and errata issues to remember
- Solo play with one hero is not great
- It can be hard to get a group to commit to a campaign
- Heroes eventually hit a ceiling and must be retired (after 33 scenarios)
- It's not cheap
- Early on, armor seems underpowered (later it gets better)
- Solo with one character is too frustrating, so I would rate that about a 4

After my first game, I wasn't all that impressed. But reflecting back, I found myself thinking more positively about it. After a couple more plays, and seeing my character start to get stronger, I was convinced. It's not my favorite game, but I'm still very much enjoying it. It's one of the few games my wife asks to play. 

There is a fair bit going on, so this is not a light or gateway game. However, it's not crazy complicated either. Once you understand it, it plays smoothly and quickly, although I wish there were fewer things to remember each scenario (or that they could all be listed in one place somehow). I agree with the BGG-voted weight of 2.7.

[c]Rules Complexity:.....:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Strategic Depth:......:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Luck Factor:..........:star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
Amount of Downtime:...:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
Fiddliness:...........:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:
Replayability:........:star::star::star::star::nostar:
Component Quality:....:star::star::star::star::nostar:
Player Interaction:...:star::star::star::star::star:
Theme Integration:....:star::star::star::star::star:[/c]

Playtime: ~75-90 minutes a scenario

Comment: Not quite sure this is 10 material, but it is damn good. The characters feel different, the locations are vastly different, weapons/spells/armor are awesome and the artwork is great. My only downside is that monsters feel a bit samey and from what I've seen there isn't a whole lot of connection between the stuff you're fighting/looting in locations. You kind of have to make up a story in your head on why you found a treasure chest in the middle of the forest for example.

I do like that death is permanent and working together is a MUST. Now, my only concern is how to sleeve this puppy.

[b]Update:[/b] Alright, after playing this game around a dozen more times I've really fallen in love with this game. I'm not even sure why, since the game essentially boils down to move, flip a card, roll some dice. There really isn't a story involved and you really have to conjure up a narrative. But god damn is this game addicting. We played it 6 times in a row which is freaking unheard of with ALL my games I've played. Only rivals Dominion in that aspect.

I do feel this game shines with 3-5 players though and 2 or 6 feels a bit lacking/constrained. It does scale well and I will easily play with 1-6, but 3-5 is the sweet spot.

I also really enjoy trying to make a narrative out of the events and creatures that come up. Some are a bit difficult to create, but others fall in place and the game shines when it does. Really hoping for other variations on the "close locations/defeat baddie" that seems to be most/all scenarios. 

Optimal Player Count: 4 >= 5 > 3 > 6 > 2 > 1
Plays equally well with all player counts, but I prefer the 3-5 range. 6 is tight, 2 and 1 is lacking unless you control 2+ characters each.

Variants Used: Slow card mix and little scenario tweaks that add more flavor. We also make death less permanent as losing a character can suck ass and jumping into a scenario is also fine.

Includes Poog of Zarongel promo and Character add-on pack.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A unique game. Pretty simple overall, but offers up some new ideas. You pick a card, you see what it is, you roll dice, and you win or lose. There are a few decisions to be made, but not a ton.

The game tries to tell a story, and sometimes succeed and other times fails. It gives you dots you can connect together, but sometimes those dots are way out of left field and ruin the suspension of disbelief.

What is interesting is the campaign and permanent death. As you play, you character acquires cards which can be used. At the end of the game, you recreate your deck using these new cars. So you're constantly adding and changing, leveling up, and becoming stronger. It's fun to watch it play out over an adventure. And it makes choices later harder because the death of your character means you're starting over.

The rules are a mess, and the errata is too many for my tastes. It's totally workable, but it's just not CLEAN in any way. It has a "play it like it makes sense" attitude, which I don't care for.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Pseudo RPG card game, where the deck-building takes place post-level as a part of your leveling process. Different quests can start to feel a little same-y. I also refuse to write on the cards to level them up, which means unwieldy printouts are necessary. The game has a lot of things that needed errata and there aren't enough cards to support all the different characters having decks at the same time.

I should also mention that thematically the game seems to work best without the character expansion pack. With just the base game, you get four different characters with four different themes that are designed to work in tandem with each other. The addon characters from the pack are hybrids and solo style people, who work against the fun of the group as a whole.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Wonderful.  A quick RPG adventure smooshed down into a card game.  Already a contender for game of the year 2013 (for me anyway).  Score could be adjusted if they can't keep it fresh with the ideas, but I'm hopeful.  Worried more about wearing the cards out than anything else.  

Update:  I scored this a 10 at first, but as I'm nearing the last adventure, I'm less compelled by it than I was initially.  Yes, it is still an RPG in thirty minutes...but it is a very fight-heavy RPG.  Most of the "role playing" happens in your head, which can be fun, but it still sometimes feels like a grind.  My two-man team is pretty much just walking through the adventures at this point and sort of getting excited when new loot shows up, but other than that, it is more "Diablo" than "Pathfinder".  
Also, Paizo's losing a point for not carrying characters over from set to set.  Extremely disappointing development.  It basically makes RotR a legacy (read: one-shot) game for me and my crew.  
Down to 8, guys.  Sorry.  I'll give the next set a try, but it'll be my last if we can't work out something to bring back our developed and, dare I say it, loved characters from older sets.

RATING: 7.5
7.5 - What I really like about this game is it captures the magic of a pathfinder RPG and gives the sense of a party working together to defeat some villain and his posse without needing a DM and I love the mini-deck building thing it has going on. A couple of minor drawbacks however are that each mission is basically the same, even though the bonuses and penalties of the locations change if you're playing with the same crew each time you'll play with the same number of locations and players and though the henchmen and bosses are different they aren't distinctive. Our regular group lost interest midway through the 4th pack and there hasn't been any desire to go back to it. It's highly repetitive.

RATING: 6.5
Innovative and exciting design. I feel like this is going to kick open the doors for others to take what's been done here, further it, and create some really great systems for matching table-top RPGs with the more streamlined approach of a boardgame..

The scenario's start to feel a bit same-samey pretty quickly, as things tend to play out similarly from one game to the next - you chase the bad-guys through various locations and hope to eventually corner and defeat the head villain - with very little variation.

I am generally left wishing there had been a bit more depth and tension to the proceedings. 

It is, however, great fun to get some of the thrills that a classic table-top RPG can offer, such as character advancement, gaining new spells, weapons and allies and just being able to watch, as you play through multiple sessions, that time and experience are truly adding up as your character becomes more and more a force to be reckoned with..

Very much looking forward to seeing the future of this system.
