RATING: 9
This is a difficult game for me to rate.  It's so much fun, but I can't figure out why.  

I love the deck management aspect of the game: Deck is your life, discarding cards is like losing HP, but many times it's necessary to do that to get to a good hand of cards that you can hold on to and use.  There's a bit of risk-reward there.  

I love the character progression between games, finding and using better weapons, items, allies and spells as you go as well as getting dice and card modifiers for various skills and your deck as you progress. It's all very cool.  

On the other hand, I've played 8 games and 4 different scenarios so far, and it's not at all difficult or challenging beating these scenarios.  There's obviously quite a bit of luck involved, but I'm hoping these scenarios get increasingly more difficult as the campaign progresses.  

Interestingly, even though the difficulty isn't really there, I still tread carefully with my decisions.  As I'm playing, I feel like the choices I make are valid and important, but at the end of the scenario, I still seem to have plenty of blessings left in the blessing (timer) deck and typically, my characters aren't on the brink of death.  That may have to do with the fact that the party I'm mainly playing with is a solo campaign with only two heroes and one is a healer.  Also, the other one got lucky and has the holy candle which can lengthen the blessings deck if needed.  

Anyway, despite the fact that the scenarios aren't tough to beat, I'm still having loads of fun playing this game.  It's strange.  Logic is telling me I should be bored with this game due to the lack of a real challenge.  I suppose in the case of PACG, there's something that I can't put my finger on.  Is it that I actually care about the characters and their progression from scenario to scenario?  I'm not sure.  There's some sense of story here, but it also has a randomness to it that takes away from a cohesive tale.  

I don't think I would ever "hate" this game, but I can't believe I love it as much as I do.

There are some issues with the rules and card errata that stops this from ever getting a 10 rating.  The lack of a real challenge brings it down another notch.  If I rated this game purely on "fun", it would probably be a 10.  For now, I think an 8 makes sense.  If this feeling of fun dissipates, my rating could drop.  If the scenarios start to become more challenging, my rating could increase.

UPDATE - Just finished Approach to Thistletop scenario in my solo campaign with Harsk and Kyra.  Beat this scenario by the skin of my teeth and the decisions became pretty tough towards the end as both characters were down to 1 - 2 cards in their decks.  Decisions about playing blessings, weapons and spells to aid the other player became very tough.  Also burning blessings to get the characters together to attempt to heal before the final battle with the villain was all very thematic, tense and fun. THIS is what I was hoping for and expecting out of this game.  My hope is that Thistletop Delve is just as tough and exciting and that the rest of the adventure packs keep with this level of strategy, theme and tension.  If they do continue in this vein, my rating may easily increase to a 9.

Update (after 17 plays) - Thisteltop Delve was crazy difficult.  I'm into Skinsaw now, loving this game - bumped rating to 9.

RATING: 8
Le jeu en lui-même est plutôt simple, voire simpliste. Le tour d'un joueur peut être décrit comme suit:
- on avance le compteur de temps
- on révèle une carte
- on effectue un jet de dés, on applique les conséquence suivant selon le succès ou l'échec.

Par conséquent, quelqu'un pourra tout à fait trouver le jeu assez ennuyeux et répétitif.
Maintenant, comme avec de nombreux jeux de cartes maintenant, chaque carte modofie légèrement les règles et ajoute un peu de saveur à l'histoire. De plus, chaque personnage a sa propre personnalité.
En outre, le système de progression des personnages est très bien fait et l'évolution de votre personnage donne beaucoup de satisfaction. Comme dans un jdr.

Jusqu'à présent je n'ai joué le jeu qu'avec des joueurs de jdr, il faudra que je le teste avec des joueurs de jeux de plateau.

________________________________________________________________________________________________

The game in itself is rather simple. One's turn can be described as follow:
- you advance the time counter
- you draw a card
- you make a dice roll, according to success or failure you execute the consequence.

As a consequence one could find the game quite boring and repetitive.
Now, as with many card games now, each card twists the rules a bit, add some flavour to the story, each character has his own personality. 
Additionally the progression of the characters is quite well done and the evolution of your character gives you a lot of satisfaction. Like in RPG.

RATING: 6
I don't mind the long setup process, but some will. 

There is strategy in how to work through the various location decks and if/when to collaborate. Characters are very different and will affect your strategy.

I think that luck outweighs strategy though. Especially how deep the henchmen are within the location decks and how early/late you encounter the villain.

Characters can keep items they've collected after a game victory, but they'll quickly accumulate the best items so future victories are more hollow.

Death seems inevitable if luck is not on your side, so your character will lose the items they fought so hard to acquire.

It's very easy to misinterpret the cards. I'm still referring to forums for clarification.

RATING: 4
This game sounds impressive.  All reviews I've watched on Youtube are positive and exciting.  I think this is mainly due to it being very new and not much actual playing going on yet.  

After playing a few scenarios (2/3 of the game as shipped), it became painfully obvious to me that the game lacks depth--big time.  There are 500 cards but really not a whole lot of variation when you actually look at what is meaningful.  If you play this game, you will understand.  Scenarios are 'different' but they are really all the same--that's not exaggeration either.  There is really no story, only a descriptive paragraph or two on adventure, scenario, and location cards.  It's just bland and definitely lacking the 'adventure' portion of the title.

The real kicker is that your success or failure is mainly determined by whether the Villain and Henchmen you are searching for in the 'location' decks are near the top or near the bottom.  If too many are near the bottom, you will run out of turns and lose.  If most are near the top, you will win absent some sort of act of God affecting your dice rolls.

The concept sounds good and probably could be good, but this game doesn't get there and I can't imagine anyone wanting to finish this game much less replay or expand their game when the expansions are available, unless you like rote, unimaginative card turning and dice rolling and then maybe this is for you.

I was frankly surprised at all the high ratings for this game after buying it (and going by those ratings in my decision to purchase it)and playing it. Perhaps boardgamers in general are very easy to impress or very hesitant to criticize.  I firmly believe with time this game's rating will drop like a rock to where it really belongs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Short review:
As a recovering video game addict I needed a solo game that gave me a RPG feel without the digital addiction. PACG is excellent. It is more abstract than a "pure" RPG, but having multiple characters and a solid story-line give a real story feel to the game. There are multiple characters to choose from to play, each of which each give a slightly different mechanic to the game so it is also re-playable.

The base game is about $50, but getting the entire game and all its expansion packs will run you a total of about $120. However, when I had completed the story with 2 groups of 3 characters each, it had taken me 2 years, making it a worth while investment. My overall cost ended up at under $1 per hour of fun: not at all bad and I'm planning on replaying the game multiple times with different storylines: there are additional stories you can get for free (or pay for) to make it re-playable, or just play again with different characters in the base game.

It can be a challenging game, if you are a casual game player, but you probably are not... Your character can die, for example. Yes, 50 hours into a 100 hour game, your character can die. You can start over, or bring them back with none of their gear or advancements (good luck...) but you are dead. You can house-rule this away if you wish, but it doesn't feel right to me if you do. A few characters died and my team left them behind and completed the story without them, but one character who died was my favorite so I restarted the game to get her back up to speed, which was depressing.

But when I actually completed the whole game with my 2 groups in the final story, it felt pretty satisfying...

Long review:
OK, so you're a grognard, die hard RPG player or just not sure about the whole game, so here it is:

The base set is $50 and comes with enough characters to play 9 different stories (called Scenarios). There are 5 other adventures numbered 2 - 6.

Each complete story is called an Adventure Path, this one is called "Rise of the Runelords", which is made up of 1 - 6. Each pack (1 - 6) is called an Adventure and each gaming session in each Adventure is called a scenario. The base set comes with 2 adventure paths, one of which has only 4 Adventures, all the rest have 5 each. Included in the box is a very short (4 scenario) adventure (explicably) labeled "B". Starting with #1 of "Rise of the Runelords", each Adventure has 5 scenarios. The box comes with B and 1. The other Adventures will cost about $15 each on sale and all together make a continuous story which you will take your group of 1 - 6 characters together to complete.

This means the complete game has 34 total scenarios. Each scenario takes around 2 hours to complete and about a 50% failure rate means the entire game would take over 100 hours to complete with 2 or 3 people playing together (or a solo player controlling them all...).

You pick a pre-made character in the box and give them gear. The magic user needs spells, the fighter needs a sword, etc. Each of these items is represented by a card, which is owned by the character. At the beginning of each scenario, your character(s) have 15 cards each to use in the game.

As you advance through the story, the characters get new powers, more available cards and abilities and increase their stats. You have some control over your characters choices in this regard, though it's usually best to play to their strengths.

Setup is kind of a bear. Lots of shuffling. I'm used to it now, but it takes getting used to. Practice, turn on some music and spend 20 minutes from unboxing to playing. The good news is, while you progress and cards go out of play, you can just put them right in the box, so cleanup is less work (but still 10 minutes or so).

You read the story, a tiny blurb on the back of the Adventure Path and Scenario cards, which set the stage for what you are trying to accomplish in the next part of the story. The adventure scenario tells you which locations to use (setup the City Gate and Apothecary, etc.) and then you put the monsters and barriers and boons (such as more and different spells, weapons, items, etc.) in the locations along with villains and henchmen and shuffle the location deck. Each character gets a turn to go to a different location, encounter the monsters and barriers, try to acquire the boons for your character and count down the timer (usually 30 turns each scenario). If you don't complete the scenario objectives in the allotted time, you lose and must replay again.

Most adventures have a villain and henchmen that are unique to that story and most of the time the goal of the story is to defeat the villain within the time limit. The stories are varied and some of them are more like a puzzle than just a simple "kill the bad guy" goal, so I found it varied and interesting. Having said that, there are now a total of 4 of the box sets ("Rise of the Runelords" is the first) and the other 3 are more challenging than this one. A serious grognard (or group of them) is likely to need to NOT play to their greatest advantage or much of the game will be too easy. If you are a grognard (serious, strategic player) you don't need to house rule to make it more challenging, simply choose some of the less optimum characters and see if you can complete the story with those. Try some roleplaying instead of just looking at the stats and it will be a challenge.

If you are on the fence about this purchase, you can try the digital version of the game for free. It is called "Pathfinder Adventures" and available on iTunes and Android.

Some other things you may want or need:
1. The "Character Add-on Pack" gives you a few extra and varied characters to play with (or to expand the game to 6 total concurrent players) as well as some varied monsters and boons (such as a very nice crossbow...) which are not present in the base game. However, this pack is by no means needed.

2. Extra dice. The dice included are good but I wanted more of them. These are cheap.

3. Ultra-Pro Clear Deck Protector Sleeves - Standard Magic the Gathering Size. The game tells you to write on the character cards in pencil, which is crazy. Just sleeve the character cards, not the whole game. I played the entire game through and never ruined a card (but I don't have kids or anything either...). As your character progresses, you can mark off the advancements you choose on the sleeve with a marker, not on the card itself.

4. Broken Token organizer for the box. I finished the entire game (all 6 adventure paths in this game with 2 groups of 3 characters each) without it, but when I bought my next box set (Skull and Shackles) I added the Broken Token and I'm glad I did.

5. There are other "Class Decks" for sale, which, if you play this game often and want to play it through multiple times, may be worth buying but they are not really needed. They are more for people who visit a friends house (or a gaming store) and want to play with their own portable character. I have a couple of these and, though good, don't add too much to the base game really. The best characters for each box set is already present in the base game (and its add on pack) so don't feel you're missing something if you don't buy any "Class Decks".

6. There are a couple of extra stories you can buy and play when you are totally done with this game. They are called The Pathfinder Society Adventure Card Guild at Paizo dot com. You buy the extra stories there and then you can buy the extra cards needed at drivethrucards dot com too, making the entire game replayable with the existing characters and cards, for a tiny investment (relatively speaking).

7. There are free stories at Welbybumpus dot com which use the existing box and cards and add a new story to the game.

8. There are, as of this review, 3 other complete adventures available: "Skull and Shackles", "Wrath of the Righteous" and "Mummies Mask". You do not continue from this adventure into those ones, they are complete adventures on their own and you start over again. You can reuse characters from Rise of the Runelords in those adventures if you wish (and vice versa) but there is no need to. Other than that, there is no real overlap between the 4 complete box sets.

9. The story is only loosely told on the scenario cards, but if you want to get more backstory, there are some well written documents on boardgamegeek dot com that you can read to get more flavor text. The one by Autoduelist is probably the best and gives you more motivation for the story.

RATING: 9
At first, I would have rated this game a 5.  I really did not like how the character card deck represented health.  It is particularly troubling that if you take damage and that makes you lose your weapons, you are pretty much screwed the rest of the scenario.  That is usually a game-ender.  However, after researching on the BGG forums I found out the real strategy is in hand/deck management.  If you manage your deck well and play your turn accordingly, you should never have to worry about losing all your weapons.  There is a lot of hidden strategy here which is not readily apparent from reading the rules.  If you are going to play this game, I highly recommend reading through the forum.

Added bonus, Paizo has the adventure/scenario card schematics available on their website.  You can create your own adventures after you complete all the ones that go with this game.

RATING: 8
Solo Player Value = Fair

Two Player Value = Fair

Three Player Value = Fair


Pathfinder offers many things that I enjoy.  Card game.  Dice rolling.  Deck pooling.  Dungeon & Dragons based game play.  Cooperative versus the game.  All of those are things that enjoy.  Yet overall this game falls flat due to a rather dull experience.

Pathfinder does have many positive factors.  The artwork is well done and the card stock is nice and thick.  Even the box is cool and has a lovely and incredibly workable insert.

The theme fits the game rather well too.  It works great with the leveling system and campaign play.

The rules are well written and learning the game from the rule book is easy.  

The campaign system is an interesting gameplay mechanic.  It adds replayability and increasing difficulty.  Playing the expansions in order is a must.  While the campaign does add a lot of replayability I don't think it make the game any more replayable once the campaign is complete.  One run through the campaign is plenty.

But after that the game gets rather dull.  Nothing is bad.  Everything is very nice.  The gameplay is tight.  It's just not exciting.

The game is basically a series of playing cards to modify, or add, dice to checks required to continue.  Each required check might be different in name, like an Enemy, or a Trap, or to find an Item, but in the end it is the same mechanic repeatedly.

This turns the game into a straight math game.  Simply do the math to get slightly above average modifiers then roll the dice.  Dull.

In the end you have a game that is fine, but a little dull, for a single run through the campaign.  But nothing more.


Pros - 
- Easy to play
- Good mechanics 


Cons - 
- Dull
- Not much beyond one campaign


Overall Playability = 6.6

Design = 6 
Strategy = 6
Luck = 7
Artwork & Components = 7
Use of Mechanics = 8
Time of Play = 7
Fun = 5
Innovation = 5
Analysis Paralysis = 8
Theme = 7


[b]-------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------[/b]


The Pathfinder expansions are simply the continuation of the campaign.  As such they are all exactly alike, although with increasing difficulty.   

Each expansion contains the same mixture of cards: enemies, items, spells, etc.  However, each version is slightly better, or more powerful, then the previous version.  Each increasing expansion offers a higher modifier, or adds more dice or some other special ability.  

This just adds to the feeling of sameness and dullness the game already suffers from.

Again, there is nothing bad or wrong here.  It is just not exciting.

It is important to play the expansions in order and only use card from the current expansion to avoid throwing off the power curve.

I will list the campaign expansions in order, but I will not go into detail on each one as they are all the same.

This is only a few more playable characters and the the associated cards to power their decks.

The added characters are nice, just not special.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
A friend of mine asked me if the game was worth buying. I ended up summarizing it as "It's a roleplaying game with all the roleplaying sucked out of it". The Skill Check mechanic (ie. add up your modifiers then roll dice to beat a number) are like those in RPG's, and the encounters feel random rather than planned out like an RPG adventure. I think that, with physical cards, you're much more limited in how much "fluff" text you can put on a card, and it's this flavor that *is* the importance of roleplaying. With roleplaying scenarios, authors have the luxury of using as many pages of text (and sometimes too much boxed text!) as they want to convey the flavorful atmosphere of RPG's. Also, despite the skill checks boiling down to rolling dice against a target number, the game has *MANY* counterintuitive or just plain odd rules that thematically don't make sense (but are necessary for game play). Finally, the later sets have improved rules and card stock -- skip to Sword and Shackle if you like pirates, and Wrath if you prefer generic fantasy.

*****

App's useful for the rules and, of course, more convenient for solo play. I really like the "card counting" the app does does. The app has a random quest version and difficulty levels that the physical game doesn't have (not that that would be hard for a fanmade variant).

The app only has Rise of the Runelords, so has *far* less content than the physical game. Plus, the app's not supported on all platforms. Don't jump in and buy stuff on the app without playing the game and seeing if it runs properly on your platform!

RATING: 7
Well worth playing if you're into deck optimizing card games with a RPG feel (and customization/development as you continue playing). Tricky to solo, until you "get" the game and find the sweet spot of how many (and which) characters to bring. Bit of a grinding game, at times, but well balanced. How to manage the dice rolls and whether to play safe right now by using a card, or taking a risk and saving the resource for later, makes for interesting choices. Even though the scenario system itself can be a bit repetitive, the short flavour texts and varied main villains actually sets the mood quite well and provides varied challenges. Character progression is rather slow, don't expect to level away after every scenario played - but on the other hand, that keeps the game balanced and challenging.

In my opinion, this has everything the Lord of the Rings LCG has, but does it better. Pathfinder makes you WANT to grit your teeth, play honestly, and earn what you get. Every small advantage actually makes a difference, and as you reduce your deck down to its starting quota of cards after scenarios, you hone your character. At first, not being able to keep all the loot can be a little disappointing, but it keeps the design clean and reins in power creep. Finding a +1 sword is a big thing, and that's how it's supposed to be!

The rulebook seems a little complex at a first read-through, and is sorely lacking an index, but once you've played a scenario or two things quickly fall into place. Still, you have to check the FAQs from time to time, and there are many small but significant misprints on the cards themselves. It's a minor gripe for me, though, the game is logical enough that I could intuit the correct solution for rule problems I encountered.

Oh, and you'll be doing a lot of deck shuffling, so sleeves are strongly recommended. They also allow you to mark character improvements without damaging the actual card (or resorting to the downloadable sheets Paizo provide on their homepage).

RATING: 10
Świetna gra symulująca trochę RPG. Genialny system rozwoju postaci pomiędzy kolejnymi scenariuszami. Sama mechanika szału nie robi, jest dość prosta i łatwa do ogarnięcia, ale gra potrafi wciągnąć, bardzo :)

RATING: 10
After playing two sessions (at the end of the second Lem slung a final, desperate spell and slew the dragon! Epic stuff!) I am very impressed with this game. 

The system of slowly evolving your character's stats and deck is simple but extremely effective and addictive. The designer of this game deserves all the accolades and glory for coming up with a way to have persistent characters and progression in a board game (ok, card game, but same thing). This is quite possibly the Holy Grail of adventure game design, and what springs from it We Shall See. Bravissimo!

I really am not keen on the LCG/expansion model for games, but I do think that anyone who buys into this will get themselves a great package and find good value for money. It seems to me that there is a hell of a lot of replayability in the base game - quite possibly in boardgaming terms infinite replayability - it's just that you are going to really want to take the adventure further and keep buying the adventure packs. If I was allowed to spend my money as I pleased (ie not married) then this wouldn't give me even a moments' pause (but then again if that was so I'd already have long since blown any cash on whatever was the hottest game/expansion series five years ago, and be up to my ears in debt, so I am not complaining, honest).

The game play itself may not be everybody's cup of tea, and that's understandable. Maybe the next game to come along which learns from this design will be able to come up with something more impressive in that area. But personally I have enjoyed the game play, and the simple but satisfying decisions and agonies over whether to play this or that card, and the little calculations about which combination of polyhedral dice gives the best chance at a positive result.

Please can somebody get the license to produce an Adventure Time version of this game, preferably standalone. I will buy that.

RATING: 5
This game is really fun at first. Chucking dice to fight monster cards and avoid traps. And then the next mission you chuck dice to fight monster cards and avoid traps. And then the next mission you chuck dice to fight monsters and avoid traps. And the next mission...okay you get the point. What starts as fun quickly becomes tedium. Flip over a card, throw dice, compare numbers, repeat, repeat, repeat. If you use all of your brain power you can at times force yourself to kind of imagine that you might be on some kind of adventure? But most of the time it's almost impossible to see any kind of theme in card flipping and number comparing.

RATING: 8
After a brief experience with RPGs during university I was looking for a similar experience in the board gaming world. PACG is the game that works exceptionally well in that regard. I have to admit the game does not tell a story in itself, it is, like its source material, a hack and slash experience where you go into a location hoping you will always stay on the holding end of various sharp objects.

There is no narrative in the game, but you can easily add the narrative yourself and the experience only gets better if you do so. If you're looking for a game that will tell you a story, similarly to Eldritch Horror, this is not your game. If you're looking for a game where every player gets to be a DM and describe the situation on the table during their turn, the number of different locations, scenarios, monsters, traps and loot to encounter will keep you entertained for weeks.

RATING: 8
[2014-03-01]
Jaj, de jó kis játék ez. Nekem végig játék közben a Mage Knighttal való hasonlóság járt a fejemben. Ez a játék abban különbözik, hogy itt nincs térkép, csak simán kártyajáték, de nagyon jó mechanizmusokkal. Mindenki egy hőst irányít, akivel különböző helyszíneken járva keresi a főgonoszt, akit nem csak legyőznie kell, hanem minden menekülési útját is el kell zárnia a végső sikerhez. Az egyes helyszíneken különböző dolgokba lehet futni, úgymint tárgyak, áldások, varázslatok (jó dolgok), illetve a főgonosz csatlósai, érdekes szörnyek és különböző akadályozó dolgok (rossz dolgok). Nagyon tetszik a karakterfejlődés. Van egy kezdeti paklink, ami az életünket is jelenti a küldetés során. Ha elfogy meghaltunk. Tehát óvatosan kell felhasználni a lapjainkat. A fejlődésnek két útja van. Az egyik a kaland megcsinálása, amivel különböző előnyök járhatnak. Lehet csak egy tárgykártya, de lehet a karakterlapunkon való fejlődés. A másik a küldetés közben talált kártyák megszerzése. Nagyon jó ötlet, hogy a küldetés végén a meghatározott számú kártyánk lehet adott fajtából. Ezért a küldetés során szerzett és alap kártyáinkból állítjuk össze a karakterünket a további feladatokra. Nagyon jól kidolgozottak a karakterek, tényleg eltérőek, különböző kártyák jók számukra. Jól kitalált az egyes tárgyak használati módja. Ha nem hozzáértő használja őket, akkor is lehetnek jók, de igazán tapasztalt kalandozó kezében működnek erősen. Nagyon tetszett ez a játék, lehet a szerepjátékos múltam miatt is, szerintem nagyon jól visszaadja az életútjátékot is. Minden játékosszámmal jó, egyedül is nagyon élvezetes lehet. Egyedül az újrajátszhatósága lehet problémás. Nagyon adja magát a kieg beszerzése és ezáltal nem túl pénztárcabarát társas.

Highly recommended!

RATING: 8
I was in two minds about this game for quite a while.  I have decided I really quite like it.  Here's why.

While I wasn't present for it, I understand there was a lot of hype around this game when it came out.  I gather that it was marketed and pushed as something of a "D&D without a DM" and it's definitely not that.  I can imagine people who wanted that getting swept up in that hype and being disappointed afterward. 

The other criticism, and to my mind, the most legitimate one, is that this game is basically just rolling dice.  You draw a card, you roll a die.  You (might) discard a card, to draw a card and roll a die.  I can understand people getting tired of that.  When your go consists of drawing a (random) card and rolling a (random) number to see if the outcome was good or bad, yeah, I can see people getting tired of that. 

I have played probably less than ten times more than five, and a few times with the digital version and I am not at all tired of this game.  

For one thing, I like rolling dice.  Especially those lovely polyhedrals I fell in love with as a child.  It's not an insignificant part of fun when playing D&D, and there you usually only roll two; d20 and whatever your weapon's damage is. 

Secondly, the choices are narrow, but that doesn't mean they are not there.  Arguably 'draw a card, play a card' is the same metric of world-famous strategy games.  I think the choices are just more subtle.  Do you risk discarding your weapon to overcome this, in hopes you heal and get it back soon?  Should you explore the waterfront, and have a greater chance of getting something cool, but suffer minuses on your attacks, or the cathedral which is safer?  How do you divvy the locations up with your party? 

I'm not denying that the game is pretty simplistic. (Though sadly not simplistic enough that you could easily bring in people new to gaming or the genre).  It is pretty simplistic.  But so are a lot of games. 

I guess there's two main things that it's fantasy.  I am a fantasy geek at heart, and I will always love using a d8 mace to take a swing at a stone golem, or a skeleton, or the bandit mastermind.  I will always love casting cure, or fireball, and picking locks and escaping ambushes.  If you choose to see the story, and imagine what's happening (and sometimes you will need to stretch your imagination to do so!) then this game is a lot of fun.  

It's a strange new experience, when everyone at the table co-creates the bizarre situation that means you failed to pick up a sword you found on the ground.  But it is strange, and it is fun, with the right people. 

Lastly, people complain that it's capricious.. because it's the roll of a dice, some days you will breeze through, and somedays you'll just get hammered.  But I love that.  I miss games where you could actually just lose and die.  You know, what yes, you got ambused, and it went badly.  There was nothing you could do.  And sometimes the gods of luck smile on you and you crush the overlord villain mage in one fell swoop.  That's life.  That's luck.  And it's harsh but that's what makes it great. 

In closing, I can see why any or all of these things might make you not want this game, and that's fair enough. I get it.  But if you are a fantasy geek like me, and you like rolling dice, and you like playing in a capricious, cruel and unfair world then I really do recommend it.  I've had a lot of fun nights with this game and I hope you do too.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I legitimately love this game.  This is a cooperative deck-builder with reasonably accessible rules, a spatial element, and one of the most refined systems of mechanics I've seen in a game since Mage Knight Board Game.

In this game each player in the party selects a character class, this character has a set of stats and powers thematically relevant to the Pathfinder RPG and a deck of cards made up of some combination of basic items, weapons, spells, allies, and blessings.  There are also stacks of cards in the center of the table that represent locations, players explore these locations and encounter monsters and traps as well as treasures they can try to acquire for their personal decks.  There is also a BBEG hiding in one of these locations, the game is won by defeating this villain when he has no other location to run to.

This game gave me a decent helping of feeling like I'm playing the tabletop RPG (and I have in fact played quite a bit of Pathfinder as well as several others) in a fraction of the time and investment the actual tabletop RPG demands.  As I said, the system is incredibly elegant.  Your deck represents your life total and functionally and mechanically each of the cards makes sense.  Some, like weapons, are simply revealed for use and have no effect on your life total but others, like spells, are discarded (without a successful check) representing exhaustion of your character.

Perhaps you noticed I typed "without a successful check", in true tabletop RPG form conflicts in this game are 99% resolved with dice rolls based on your character's combat prowess and skill and ability set.  A character's stats are thematically balanced with their class along with their starting deck make-up.

Finally, the game can (and in my opinion, should) be played as a continuous campaign.  At the end of an adventure your character levels up gaining stat bonuses and powers as well as customizes their deck with the new loot they found in the previous adventure.  The adventures get progressively more difficult and intense but characters level up and become more skilled and better equipped to meet those challenges.

It's not ENTIRELY puppy-dogs and rainbows though, I have played this game with 6 players (with the character add-on pack) and that was a bit much.  It did extend the playing time a little, but not excruciatingly so, and there was down time but that can be remedied by really getting into the game and looking over your cards and seeing how you can help your team mates.  I feel like in my 6-player game a few of the players checked out when it wasn't their turn.  Even with dedicated players though 6 people brings with it the most number of locations and different characters with varied decks and especially for early plays the very varied abilities on all these cards can be overwhelming.  I constantly forgot about powers and abilities of cards including my own character card throughout that game while trying to take everything in.

I feel like 4, the max with just the base game, is probably the sweet spot for this game and there is a lot of content in just the base set which includes the first adventure module.  There are many more adventure modules in the pipeline so this game is going to have a lot of replay value.  One I'm certainly champing at the bit to play again and I don't expect to get tired with the amount of varied content and gameplay this offers.

RATING: 3
I never went into this expecting an RPG experience. I did, however, expect it to be a light fun card game.

Initial impressions were high. Conceptually, I almost love it. Locations that are standardized enough to maintain continuity, but fluid enough to allow variety. Also, being able to take a character and improve it from session to session sounded really cool.

Repeated plays, however, revealed it to be somewhat samey and repetitive. Also, the design doesn't feel particularly tight. The game engine has some loose screws. The rules aren't the worst I've ever read, but they are far from airtight.

Best thing about this game is that it's so popular I was able to trade it for Tash-Kalar.

RATING: 6
Really mixed bag here. Gameplay is very simple. Draw a card, roll what it tells you to. There are a lot of cards, but most are just variants on the same thing; the game has some 16 odd stats, which differ only in when a card you draw tells you to roll them.
As a one-off game, this would be dull fast. The appealing part is the campaign and character advancement, and co-op play. When we got more into that, we're having a lot of fun with it.
Several rules are very, very confusing. No, they go past confusing. Many of the rules and much of the card text is just flat out wrong. This lowers my rating a bit.
The variety is very limited, and they designed it to lack replay value so they can churn out expansions. If you are willing to buy all the expansions, there's a lot of gameplay here.
Not sure how to rate it; probably closer to a 7 or 8 for the whole series, but we wouldn't play the base set much on its own.

RATING: N/A
I was interested in this but having seen how the game is designed I can't see any reasons to play it.

The focus of the game is grinding. Grind through this scenario, get some loot, grind through the next scenario, get some loot, character dies, grind a new one to a competitive level, new expansion is out? grind through some more scenarios. 

Grinding sucks but it can be tolerated if there's good game mechanics to keep things interesting. Here its all flip a card, calculate probabilities if not obvious, roll some dice. Oooh dice! Rolling that 6 really convinced that villager to help us. So thematic.
 
And it's even more tolerable if there's a story and interaction to keep you interested. What's that, no story at all? Well how about NPC interaction? No none of that either. Well how about inter-player interaction? Nope. Well you can add a small bonus here or there but that's about all. Can't let that any of that nonsense get in the way of grinding for loot.

One of the reasons I don't play RPG videogames is that I don't have time for their nonsense. Why would I then play a slower, less interesting version of that. Yes it's more social but only because people are in the same room. In game there's zero. Also the difficulty of getting the same group of friends around regularly enough to make it through a season is nigh on impossible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Roleplaying games are all about the stories they tell. This card game tries to simulate that and add in 'game mechanisms'. In the same way as Lords of Waterdeep before it, I feel as though it loses a lot of the appeal from storytelling and adds very little in the way of interesting mechanisms.

In our first play one of our players spent almost the entire game evading cards (read, putting them at the bottom of the pile of cards) and so really didn't do *anything* for the entire game. Yes, you're meant to play the same characters throughout a series of 'adventures' and so each character will have varying amounts to do in different types of mission, but as a single play, that's just poor design.

As for the game itself; flip a card. You are required to roll an 8 to beat this card. You get a D8. Would you like to another D8? If so, play this card (okay). Would you like to add a D4? (okay). Now your team mates ask if you need any more help - "Well," I say, " my outcome is anything from 3 to 20 and I have no control over it, so who knows?" With no post-roll modifiers, this is all just about managing probability and risk and not in a fun way at all. We played Can't Stop earlier in the day. It offered all of the same decision making in a third of the time and with 1% of the components.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Play twice: Brigadoom solo (2 char), and first 3 scenarios with 3-players.

My issues are that one, there was no narrative juice to suck out. [Parse that NSA!] Two, given that character and toy advancement are fundamental to the experience, there is a profound disconnect between the consequences of dying (lose essentially everything you've gained over many many hours of play), vs voluntarily failing the mission to protect a character in danger. There is no downside to failing a mission other than the time you sunk into that one short encounter (though I think you can keep whatever you've already found in that failed mission).

I think the "deck is your life" is a cool idea. What I don't like about Pathfinder Card Game is that it has a "once through" mechanism, rather than cycling as in Decipher's Star Wars CCG,  or deck "refreshing" as in Middle Earth Quest. You draw through your deck and you die. Yes, there can be opportunities to heal (refresh: discards to draw pile), but in the early going, the upside seems incredibly meager vs the opportunity cost.

Worse than once through, your character is in threat of perma-death unless you have N cards in your draw pile, where N is your hand size. With starting decks of 15 cards and hand size of 6, you only have access to 9 cards before you're at risk of dying. You can safely draw 3 cards. Weeee.

There are some tricks you can pull to put cards in hand under your draw pile. At least some low level characters have a very limited means of searching their deck for a crucial card. Yes, each character gets a single, guaranteed starting card, but that's not always enough. The cleric doesn't start with a weapon, and without it you're kind of terrible. If your mace is buried, drawing cards to search for it is quickly suicidal. Frustrating and non-thematic.

"Oh hey guys, you know those villagers who are about to get slaughtered? Let's just let that happen. We can saunter back to the tavern, hope I can find my mace, and start this all over. I'm sure there will be some survivors that we can, um, save. Tomorrow."

We didn't actually face that problem, because we won fairly handily all 3 times. In that sense there was little tension. It felt like a puzzle, with a fairly substantial setup time, mitigated only by an all hands on deck approach to scenario construction. There is a potential danger of it being very rinse-and-repeat, but we didn't continue long enough to test that. 

With all that said, I feel like it's a huge near miss. It could have been awesome. Many people do think it's awesome. Maybe I'm missing something, and the idea is so tantalizing that I want to give it another shot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
A co-op game I really like. Probably because there isn't always an "obvious leader driven" solution. Its not a deckbuilder in the sense of Thunderstone, Ascension or Legendary. Rather, your "deck" is your HP and its filled with skills, allies, weapons, and such. There is a nice balance between sort of "digging" for useful things and not dying :) - the deck builder comes from acquiring new cards  during a scenario and then sifting through the loot to rebuild your deck for the next adventure.

Also, each character really plays fairly differently in how you use them. having played 8 or 9 of the classes, each approach has to be different and can be tweaked a bit depending on how you like to use them (of course, the equipment you find makes a big difference too).

If there is any one downside to the game, it is that it is a MASSIVE card game and setting up and tearing it down is a pain in the ass. Yes, the insert is sweet, but still...

Overall, this is a card game that feels an awful lot like you are running through an adventure campaign.

**Update Jan 2017
Having played through the entire game - IN THE iOS APP, I have mixed feelings about the tabletop game. First, the above description is still true and this is a fun game, but the setup and teardown is a bit much. There is a good progression through the game's adventures/campaign, though the story is more of a "fill in the details with your own imagination" thing (the app does fill in those story gaps, but only a hair more). 

My biggest complaint though is Paizo and Obsidian (the app developer). Their purchase model is basically the same costs for the physical vs the app. I'm not really sure I want to do this again with the other sets (and man are there a lot of sets out there). The game was fun, but doesn't draw me out enough to pay for another set - not when I can play the app and avoid setup and teardown and having to keep track of everything.

RATING: 4
A single learning game is hardly enough to rate this game, so consider mine a tentative score at best.

This co-op feels closer in spirit to Sentinels of the Multiverse than to, for example, Pandemic. The reason I say this is because the game is very much about working to overcome a series of individual bad guys (or obstacles that are mechanically equivalent to bad guys) as efficiently as possible as opposed to solving a single larger puzzle. If you are looking for a "roleplaying" experience in a board game, I think you'll be better served by one of the D&D co-ops or something more traditional like Descent. Pathfinder is fine for what it is, but doesn't satisfy any desire that I have.

What may up my rating is the campaign play, which I haven't had a chance to experience. In its long form, Pathfinder may reveal strategic choices that increase my enjoyment (and therefore my rating).

RATING: 6
Translation is horribly bad. There are lots of cards with misleading or unclear translations, references to keywords that are nowhere to be found in the rules book and sometimes even contrary to what was intended. Additionally the game is astonishingly fiddly to set up with pulling out all the selected cards and building the rather non-thematic location decks. It's kinda weird in that way, because even though it has a rather bland mechanics and fiddly setup, when you get going, you want to get through and finish to distribute the loot. And play again, because you build up your character. But you really have to bring your own story, because the game itself doesn't give you much of that. In a way that's very close to old-style RPG play, where the story didn't come from pre-built modules, but from what the GM and the players could come up with. Box includes the character addon. The expansion boxes (both the addon and the included first expansion) are damaged, but all cards are fine and complete.

RATING: 5

I was curious about Pathfinder, and got a chance to play.  I only played a one-off scenario, and I suspect the true joy of the game is the campaign mode which lets you level up and acquire new cards and make persistent changes to your character.  That would be where the main appeal of this game would lie for me... being able to customize and choose a direction and flavor for my character.  My favorite part of any fantasy based game has always been setting up the character initially and leveling up and assigning skill points.

The artwork in the game is good, and I like the detail and minutiae on all the cards.  And I like the idea of the game. Unfortunately, the execution of the game just felt like random dice rolling. Our game followed the same basic format throughout the game.  Pick a location, explore as much as you can and hope you roll high dice.  There were occasionally strategic decisions where you had the ability to influence what type and how many dice you roll and whether you assisted other players with blessings, etc. but for the most part those were fairly obvious decisions.  Maybe it didn't help that I played with people who announced things like "You're expected value is an eleven."  So there were turns when things went against us and complaints were leveled against players who "rolled poorly".  And we had victories when players rolled well.  But other than the joy and dismay of the high and low dice rolls, there wasn't much else going on.

It wasn't an unpleasant way to spend 45 minutes... but it didn't really feel engaging either.  I feel like I could have stepped away at any point and asked someone else to roll the dice for me on my turn and there wouldn't have been any significant difference from how I would have played the game, regardless of how long it took me to get back.  On the other hand, it's a game my son would probably love.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[thing=133038][/thing] is a light, cooperative game, set in the world of medieval fantasy (yes, basically D&D). The dynamics of the game is quite simple: each character has an especific amount of cards that must be in his deck. For instance, Harsk, the dwarf ranger, that prefers tea to beer, must have in his deck: 5 blessings, 5 weapons, 1 armor, 3 itens and 1 ally, making his 15 card deck - this number is the same for every character (at least in the base game), what changes is the amount of the type of cards, and also the type itself.

Once the deck of the heroes are ready, is necessary to prepare the decks of the locations: each scenario card indicates which locations will be used, and each location card has the info regarding how to build the deck for it: usually it takes 9 cards + 1 scenario card. The 9 cards come from different types: monsters, barriers, blessings, weapons, spells, allies, and so on. As you can see, some of these types matches the ones the players can have in their decks - and if the player finds these cards, they can take it (if he passes in a test), even if he doesn't have a use for it, as he can passes the card to his companions (during the scenario, or later, if they won the scenario).

After the locations decks are ready, players decide in which location his adventurer will explore. Then, in order, the player first reveal a blessing from the time deck (I call it time deck - it is the in game clock to mark how much time is left for the adventurers to complete the scenario). Then he explores, flipping the first card of the location deck and dealing with it (usually the card will require a test - sometimes to kill a monster, or to sneak by it; or to acquire an item; or to avoid/pass a barrier; and so on). It is possible to continue exploring, if the player can discard a blessing or an ally - but if he doesn't have neither one, or he doesn't want to, the turn passes to the next player. This goes for up to 30 turns, or sooner, if the adventurers win the scenario (or everyone dies).

Each character has the same attributes, but in different values (or different specializations) - Harsk rolls a d6 for Strength, a d8 for Dexterity (with +3 for Ranged), a d12 for Constitution, and so on. This makes them more apt to deal with some situations and locations - and it is up to the players to figure this out.

The game is basically this, the rest is just follow the instructions on the cards, and consider which cards to use and when to use them. After the scenario, the players can change the cards in the deck of the adventurer, but they can't change the amount of cards (so Harsk can take a better bow, but he can't have 6 weapons in his deck - one of the other weapons must go).

The game has some level of interaction between the players, more than in [thing=30549][/thing], but less than you get in [thing=42776][/thing], for instance. And the game might have more interaction than I give for it, mostly because the adventurers chosen in my group don't really do team-work - Harsk, the dwarf, is the most helpful to others! It is probably something in the tea.

RATING: 6

There is still some distance left to the full rpg experience!

Also, mechanically the core card play mechanic could have been perhaps a bit better structured. Now it can be a bit confusing when and how you can play your cards.

All in all, I found this game OKish.

RATING: 3
Bought into the hype here, sadly... I agree with much of what was said here: http://www.shutupandsitdown.com/blog/post/review-pathfinder-adventure-card-game/

It's a dull exercise in moving to a location, rolling some dice, then waiting five minutes to do the same thing all over again. It has no narrative and no interesting decisions. Furthermore the graphic design is incredibly bland and the card quality is mediocre.

Also, I resent that they couldn't give you ALL the characters for the MSRP of $60.

I don't understand how this monster of hype is still moving up the BGG charts. Ugh. I think people want it to be better than it is and rate based on wishful thinking.

RATING: 7
Another reason why I like this as opposed to the LCG type games (LotR, Game of Thrones, Arkham Horror). You may have to buy other Base Sets to play more than 2 players. To play other scenarios, you seem to have to become familiar with a large chunk of cards to know how to build your deck. 

Here, your deck is small, the number of cards you gain is also small. You see small changes in your character as they get better items, better stats, larger decks(HPs). I don't have to ask my friends to buy a Basic Set if they want to play it. (We were playing MtG back in the v2/v3 days and are very much done with the CCG scheme).

My friends S&D also seem to like this game.  We barely won our first Adventure (on the last blessing card!).

RATING: 6
A neat system, in a pretty crowded genre.  Deck building as a campaign is neat, and it's really quite cool how well they managed to make the typical RPG elements fit in here, between the different locations, different types of cards, and different classes.  Without a DM, you miss the storytelling aspect, but if you just like killing stuff for loot, this is a great fit.  It really does feel different depending who you are.  There's potential here to get a lot out of this if you're willing to run a long-term campaign.  You just might get really attached to a character.

RATING: 10

Really enjoy that this lets you play a DnD/Pathfinder-style game with hard structure and no DM. It can be a little expensive to play through to the end given that you have to purchase the later scenarios, but I enjoyed the game from start to finish and with the variety of characters the game has decent replay value. Rules and card actions can sometimes be a bit ambiguous but for the most part it's pretty clear.

A+ to Paizo for continuing to support Pathfinder ACG on an ongoing basis by releasing new base sets as well as the character class decks that can be played with any of the base sets. These decks are fun (gnome summoner with demonic chicken cohort? amazing) and further add to game's replay-ability.

The game also functions well for solo play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is like an A++ for proof of concept for deck building campaign system, but sort of a fail as an interesting game. There is a fairly deep hand management aspect to the mechanics, but the actual adventuring is pretty random and mundane. It pretty quickly devolves into flip the top card and roll a die, then either pass or fail. Wash, rinse, repeat. It's pretty lame if you look to hard.

Still, the end of game wrap up where you divvy up the loot and build your deck for the next session is awesome. The idea of playing a character over multiple campaign games is awesome. Ditto to character perms-death. It really is a game changer. I hope this mechanic shows up again, tightened up, improved and married to other proven card and board game mechanics. Then they will be onto something.

RATING: 8
2013-09-09
Collecting new loot, recruiting allies, and hunting down villains are great. Playing a Rogue doesn't quite match the usual role one plays in an actual D&D game, though. Whereas in an actual pen and paper RPG, a Rogue could get an attack bonus simply for sneaking up on an enemy already occupied with another party member, here there is no possibility for such a bonus unless you're going it alone. Also, other than the ability to use some blessings on other characters, the Rogue has very little support to provide. With the random encounter setup, if another player stumbles across a trap or some other obstacle that is traditionally Rogue territory, you can't just step in and say, "hey, let me handle that" since it gets shuffled back into the deck.

But, despite the slight change in approach for a sneak-thief, this is a fantastic game. Having a tabletop RPG-like game without requiring someone to take on the extra workload of being a DM makes this a lot easier to jump into - and continue - than the traditional counterpart.

RATING: 8
The Lord of the Rings Card Game is a great family coop game, but it's very thoughtful and mathy.  If you want something lighter and dice-based, Pathfinder is the way to go.

Still, I'd play either game anytime. Though LOTR may ultimately have more longevity due to the special rules and cards that change things drastically from scenario to scenario.

One pro in favor of Pathfinder is its campaign system.  I love the advancement method of swapping out old cards for new cards between adventures.

[i]Update:[/i]

The family loves this one. Rating increases a point.

Also, the temporary closure and villain vanquishing mechanics are extremely clever.  A really well-designed game, overall.

[i]Update #2:[/i]

I don't know what's beyond "love" on the game appreciation scale, but that's where my family has settled.  Pathfinder is their favorite game ever.

If you're thinking of giving PACG a whirl, I've typed up advice for new players [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1780710/advice-new-players]here[/url].  Enjoy the ride!

RATING: 10
I know.  A 10...  You might be thinking, fanboy.  No, this gets a 10 for me because this game is fun for what I want out of it.  Looting, check.  Character development that carries from game to game, check.  

Not a lot of board games give you a feeling of Pen & Paper Role Playing Games like D&D--where you keep track of your character and advance them over time.  This game does it.

Easy? Yeah, it is.  I don't fault the game for it.

Some criticize it for being boring.  My advice is take a break from it.  Play something else.  When you get the itch to kill things and loot them, PACG will be waiting for you.

RATING: 10
Currently my favorite solo game due to theme, playtime, and fun factor. 

I've played through 3 scenarios solo and have really enjoyed it. I like the character building aspect and have comes across some very amusing and tense situations with my "spear wielding warrior." Very excited to try a 3 player campaign with friends. Perfect for solo play. Also very simple to learn and teach others. Scratches that "RPG" itch.

Unfortunately lacks story; was really hoping for a more thematic experience...but not a big deal. I've got a good imagination. 

I'll have a better opinion of the game when I play multiplayer. I do see a repetitive nature to the game...would've liked to have seen a few twists or game altering cards put into play.

Cards are decent quality; though the black borders are going to wear terribly and I dont sleeve =/

RATING: 6.75
Excelente juego las primeras 10 partidas, luego se vuelve repetitivo, la mecanica de mejorar permanentemente a tus personajes (via renovando cartas debiles por mejores cartas y con los checks de skills) es una mecanica de lo mejor. A largo plazo se disfruta mas el armado de tu deck y el metajuego que el juego en si.

Arte impresionante, lo recomiendo si te gustan los juegos de aventuras lo pruebes al menos el primer arco de la caja base. Se juega bien solo y creo aun mejor de 2 en cooperativo.

Imprescindible que si se juega descargues los prologos y epilogos del bgg user:kittenhoarder (https://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/1199461/rise-runelords-prologues-and-epilogues)

A ver si Mike Selinker mejora el sistema de juego en su nuevo juego de 2017 Apocrypha

RATING: 9
This game is likely to be a perfect 10 for me as soon as I start playing the adventure packs. I adore it, and have only played it solitaire so far.

The game has the finest form of deck building that I have ever come across. I love how you may add a few cards after each scenario, but must keep the same ratio of cards, unless you've earned a card feat. Great stuff!

I also enjoy the theme, even with the lack of fluff text beyond the adventure path, adventure, scenario, and location cards. This is a hit.

RATING: 2
I like cards, I like dices, I like fantasy theme, I love dungeon crawls, I like deckbuilders, I love exploration, I love co-op.

I hate the millions of skill names which doesn't make any sense at all (fortitude, diplomacy... really?), I hate monsters with zero flavor or meaningful abilities, I don't feel like I´m fightning at all, actually I feel zero pressure in this game aside from not being able to play my cards, I hate non-thematic closing locations (?), I hate getting marginally better cards than the ones I have in my hand already, I hate being forced to keep my plays in hand, I hate that I have to pay for playing my cards, I hate that being the mage I only have three spell cards in the deck and they´re not even good, I hate that I finally get to upgrade and all there is to do really is to +1 a card because no other choice is even remotely as good as that, I hate that we never have cards allowing me to interact with my fellow heroes despite the rulesbook says everywhere how we can help each other, I hate the blessing deck which has exactly zero point other than being a timer (and the blessings of the gods often do the same thing as the blessing card anyway), I hate long setup time, I hate the fact the game is summed up to a turn up card roll dice if you fail nothing happens kind of game, it's brainless and not very fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
I need to experience more of what it offers, before settling into a solid opinion on this game.

What I've seen thus far sets it leagues ahead of Mage Knight, in terms of enjoyment and nuts and bolts design - that said, I pretty much agree with all of Rich Moore's comments:

1. This is what people who don't play RPGs, think a RPG should be (all dice throwing and skill checking).
2. There's no narrative.
3. There's barely any decisions to make.
4. It's a rinse-and-repeat game, over and over again.

RATING: 9
Played this for the first time on 4/16/2014. It was a blast which we all loved. We played three of us and we all had a fantastic time. My wife liked it also very much though she isn't a roleplayer. This is a big plus in my book. Though some perhaps say that the frequent use of dice makes this a luck based game, I cannot agree with this. Making your cards work together and tweaking your deck is what mitigates luck for a very BIG part.

It sure is going to see a lot of plays at our house!

Upped this to a "9" now, what a fantastic game!

RATING: 9.5
\begin{High_pitched_voice} AWESOME!!! \end{High_pitched_voice}

This is definitely one of the best game I played in a long time! 
It's not perfect (see below), but it gives me the same feeling of an RPG with the advantage that I can play it with my wife when I have a spare hour.
Unfortunately, the production issues between first and second printing are a little bit annoying (especially since I have to buy the game online and I have no control on the printing version of the item I'm buying). Nevertheless, if you are willing to close one eye on the production issues and on the never-ending FAQ/Errata, the game plays very well and is extremely engaging. This is one of those games that, as soon as you are done with a scenario, you want to immediately play the following one!

RATING: 2
Picked up this game, and was enamored by the concept and potential for cool *game*y fun (I actually do enjoy even a good game of abstract solitaire, so even mechanical theme-less games draw me sometimes).  
But all this game is is a disappointment.  After only 4 or 5 rounds, it became perfectly clear that all I was doing was flipping a card, rolling a dice to see if I could get higher than the number on the card, then either taking are discarding said card accordingly - rinse, repeat.  There wasn't even lather!  
Maybe if I had preciously played the Pathfinder RPG and Runelords story line, this *might* have served as a pleasant reminder of it.  But even the, all you do is roll a dice to beat one random number and move on.  There is pretty much no decision making here.  It's a-kin to blackjack.  either "hit-me" or "stand".  
And it's a shame, because the idea of expanding your characters repitware of cards over multiple playthroughs seems like it could have been fun.  But, unless i'm really not playing the game right, nothing that happens on ANY of the cards amounts to more than a *skill check* dice role with no interesting card interactions, player interactions, tactics, strategy.  And NO story.  Sure, you can read each adventure card to GET a story.  But, why not just read those and skip the game?  it would go from being "1% story and no game, just luck", to "100% story with no game and no frustrating luck".  
This *game* wouldn't even make a passable choose-your-own-adventure.
PASS

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I'ld recommend to buy this game on steam during sales. It's a lot cheaper than getting the paper version. The game is addictive in that you'll always be looking forward to upgrading your deck with new cards. 
Basically there's not much depth to the game. It's about risk and card management. There's also a luck factor in rolls and finding the bosses in the decks before the timer runs out. The character progression is nice and the characters feel different enough, though there're a lot of secondary stats you'll not use often. I like the pc version because you don't have to go through the rules and there's no setting up the whole thing, so you can easily play for a half hour per evening for some quick relaxing adventuring fun.

RATING: 5.7
Very confused by this game. It manages to emulate the skinner box addiction of numerous electronic RPGs and is as equally devoid of narrative as those same leveling-focused games tend to be... yet it's amazingly enjoyable. I guess at the end of the day if it's fun, maybe it's best to just not ask so many questions.

Edit: 12/23/14
The barren nothingness of this game is now readily apparent after getting back into true blue pen and paper roleplaying games. Just not worth my time.

RATING: 10
While the rulebook could definitely use some improvements, this game is a hit!  I particularly enjoy the character progression, as long-term character investment is one of my favourite aspects of RPGs. 

The mechanisms of this game are quite fun.  Sure, they're on the lighter side, but they still work well overall.  I'm looking forward to future adventures, while equally excited about repeatedly replaying what's already available.
